4.20       Civil Works.  ICB, in accordance with Bank guidelines, would be
used for the procurement of civil works to reconstruct part of MARNDR's
headquarters building estimated to cost US$0.7 million. Domestic bidders for
such would be granted a margin of preference of 7.51. Other civil works,
e.g., school facilities in various places, water development subprojects, the
rehabilitation of the access and internal roads for the La Visite National
Park and in the Pine Forest Reserve and buildings in the national parks,
estimated to cost between US$50,000 and no more than US$250,000 each, and in
total not more than US$2.1 million, would be procured on the basis of local
competitive bidding procedures acceptable to IDA. Because of the minor scope
of these works and their scattered location, they would not attract
international contractors. Small works such as for nurseries and guard
houses, estimated to cost less than US$50,000 each and in total not more than



- 29 -
US$0.6 million, would be procured by local shopping procedures acceptable to
IDA involving at least three quotations.  Vehicles andt Acuitmnt.  Vehicles9
estimated to cost about US$1.5 million in total, would be procured through ICB
in lots of at least US$500,000 each. Equipment contracts estimated to cost
more than US$100,000 each and US$0.6 million in total, would also be procured
under ICB. Small equipment contracts, estimated to cost between US$15,000 and
US$100,000 each, and US$0.2 million in total, would be procured under LCB
procedures acceptable to IDA. Equipment contracts estimated to cost less than
US$15,000 each and not exceeding a total of US$0.1 million, would be procured
using local shopping procedures acceptable to IDA, based on at least three
price quotations. Consultants. IDA-financed consultants, including NGOs,
would be hired in accordance with the Auaust 1981 Guidelines-for the Use of
Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by The World Bank as Executing Agency
(para. 5.05).
4.21        All procurement activities under the project would be administered
by DRN's project unit headed by the project administrator and the financial
manager. Prior review and comment by IDA would be required for: (i) all ICB
procurement; (ii) all LCB contracts for civil works estimated to cost more
than US$100,000 each; and (iii) all LCB equipment contracts estimated to cost
more than US$50,000 each.
Disbursement
4.22       The proceeds of the proposed IDA credit would be disbursed over
seven and one half years, in line with the disbursement profile of June 1991
for agriculture sector projects in Haiti against the following categories of
expenditure, all of which would be disbursed at 100% of expenditure, except
for imported items procured locally under category (b) which would be
disbursed at 85% of local expenditure: (a) contracts for civil works;
(b) equipment and vehicles; (c) increment.l salaries and allowances;
(d) recurrent costs; (e) consultants services, scholarships and overseas
training; (f) extension consultants; (g) water development subprojects; and
(h) refunding of a US$750,000 PPF. Categories (b), (d), and (e) would each be
divided between expenditures to be made by the DRN and the BME.
4.23       Disbursement against incremental salaries and allowances
(category (c)) would be subject to annual ceilings as follows:

Accounts and Audit
4.27       Assurances were obtained at negotiations that:  (i) separate
subproject accounts would be maintained by DRN and BHE to reflect all project
expenditure; (ii) these accounts would be audited annually by independent
auditors acceptable to IDA, with special opinion expressed on the statements
of expenditures; (iii) the audited accounts for each fiscal year would be
submitted to IDA no later than six months after the close of the fiscal year;
and (iv) additional audits of more limited scope, including a statement by the
auditors on the compliance with the covenants under the Development Credit
Agreement, would be prepared and submitted quarterly to IDA (para. 7.02 (h)).
V. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
Organization and Management
5.01       Overall Coordination.  The principal organizational units involved
in the project include: (i) the Ministry of Economy and Finance as the
Borrower of the credit on behalf of Haiti; (ii) the MARNDR with its
Directorate of Natural Resources and the Forestry and Environmental Protection
Services; and (iii) the BME. Responsibility for the day-to-day coordination,
including the preparation end supervision of work programs, reporting and
overall technical aspects would rest with the implementing agencies. A
project administration unit located within DRN would coordinate the
institutional strengthening and the forestry and environmental programs in
HARNDR. The Director of the BME's Energy Sector Management Assistance
Project, assisted by a full-time conservation coordinator (para. 4.11) would
be responsible for the management and coordination of the household energy
conservation subproject.
5.02       Ministry of Agriculture. Natural Resources and Rural Develonment.
Management of the forestry and environmental protection components of the
project would be delegated by the Minister to the Director of Natural
Resources in the MARNDR. The management capacity of the office of the
Director of the DRN would be reinforced by a deputy director and a project
unit composed of a project administrator and two advisors recruited for the



- 31 -
duration of the project through an international firm of management
consultants, counterpart and support staff, transport, equipment and new
office facilities (para. 4.05). DRN would interact with other agencies of the
NARNDR that would be affected by project activities, in particular its
Director General who has the responsibility for MARNDR's field offices, and
the Director for Administration, who is responsible for staff recruitment and
administration. It was agreed during negotiations that: (i) project
implementation and financing would be on the basis of detailed annual work
programs and budgets that would be prepared by the project's implementing
services and compiled by DRN; (ii) DRN would submit to IDA the proposed annual
work programs and budgets no later than July 31 of each year for IDA's review
and comment; and (iii) advise IDA of any subsequent addition or modifications
in the program content, cost and scheduling and seek IDA approval
(para. 7.03 (i)). It was also agreed during negotiations that any changes to
the Organic Law of MARNDR that would materially and adversely affect the
project, will constitute an event of suspension (para. 7.05).
5.03       The day-to-day implementation of the annually specified work
programs of the DRN subproject would be the responsibility of the service
chiefs for Forestry and Environmental Protection, who would report to the
Director of the DRN. Within the services, specialized sections, whose heads
report to the Service Chiefs, would perform specific activities of the
forestry and environmental programs. The project would provide the material
and human resources to strengthen these two services and those sections that
implement project supported programs. These sections already exist, except
the proposed new section for cartography and information services in the SPE,
but are understaffed and underfunded.
5.04       Bureau of Mines and Energy.  Similar to DRN within the MARNDR, BME
would operate as part of the Ministry of Public Works, Transport and
Communications, implementing through its Energy Sector Management Assistance
Project and technical support from the project management firm or other
sources, the household energy conservation subproject on the basis of annual
work programs and budgets (para. 7.02 (i)). BME's management capacity would
also be strengthened by the appointment of a full-time conservation
coordinator (para. 4.11), a two-year assignment of a locally recruited energy
economics and planning advisor, vehicles and equipment, and short-term
consultants (paras. 4.11 and 4.13 (e)).
Technical Assistance and Consultancies
5.05       Project implementation would be ri cilitated by a large number of
both long- and short-term domestic and internationally recruited concultants.
A listing of project financed consultants, and studies and reports that would
be prepared under the project is in Annex 10, Chart 4. To the maximum extent
possible, technical specialists required for project implementation would be
hired by the firm selected as management consultants for the project
(para. 4.05 (a)). In order to avoid conflicts of interest, consultants for
monitoring and supervision functions, such as for the annual monitoring of the
environmental approaches and impact of project activities, as well as for the
preparation of the mid-term and end-evaluations of the project, would not be
recruited through the management firm (paras. 5.07 and 5.08). It was agreed



32 
during negotiations that the selection and appointment of the management firm,
and the specialists recruited through the firm, as well as individually hired
consultants or other firms/agencies/N4GOs, would be consistent with the
Aguaut 1981 Guidelines -for the Use-of Consultants bv World Bank Borrowers and
bv the World Bank as Executing Agency (paras. 4.20 and 7.02 (c) (i)).
Monitoring, Progress Reporting, and Evaluation
5.06       Continuous monitoring of the MARNDR project activities would be
carried out by the project unit in DRNI, which would undertake corrective
actions, advise the implementing units on necessary actions, and inform higher
levels of Government on project issues and performance. DRN's project unit
would prepare and submit to IDA by May 30 and November 30 of each year
semiannual progress reports on all activities carried out under the project,
including the household energy conservation subproject, based on the
individual reports from the implementing agencies and services both in DRN and
BME. The progress reports would compare actual project implementation with
the approved Annual Work Programs and Buugets9 and highlight issues and
problems of project implementation and outline possible corrective actions,
for comment by the Government, IDA and monitoring agencies.
5.07       To monitor the project's environmental aspects and support
implementation of the environmental protection prigrams, an experienced and
highly respected external agency, such as the University of Florida, which is
involved in implementing a USAID supported natural park management program in
Haiti, would 'be hired by DRNo This agency, through its specialized staff and
consultants, would provide comments and recommendations on the annual work
programs and budgets, review and comment on progress reports, and participate
in project evaluations. Agency staff would travel to Haiti at least once a
year during the third quarter to coincide with the preparation of the Annual
Work Programs.  As part of the project's Implementation Program, it was agreed
at negotiations that DRN would hire the external monitoring agency by March
31, 1992, under terms and conditions acceptable to IDA (para. 7.02 (c) (vi)).
5.08       The Government, with assistance from independent consultants, would
also carry out a mid-term review of on project performance, the results of
which would provide the basis for necessary adjustments in project design and
funding allocation. It was agreed during negotiations that the mid-term
evaluation would be completed by March 31, 1994 (para. 7.02 (k)). To
accomplish this: (i) independent consultants to assist in the evaluation
would be hired by October 1, 1993; and (ii) the draft report would be
available for review by IDA by February 1, 1994. As a standard requirement of
IDA lending, the Borrower shall also prepare a Project Completion Report at
the end of the project investment period.
5.09       Project supervision b- IDA would require a larger than usual staff
input, estimated at about 18 staffweeks on mission per year (see Annex 10,
Chart 2)o Regular supervision missions should include an
agriculturalist/forester/environmentalist, a financial analyst, and an energy
planner.

VI. PROJECT BENEFITS. INPACT AND RISKS
enefits
6.01       The project wo-ld generate benefits froms  (a) timber production as
a result of the management of the Pine Forest5 (b) incremental crops and tree
production as a result of the agroforestry extension services; 'c) savings of
charcoal consumption as a result of the promotion of more efficient stoves;
and (d) institutional strengthening that would pave the way 7or Haiti to
oxpand its programs to improve its natural resource base.
6.02        The Pine Forest incremental production, both from existing stands
and plantations or natural regeneration and estimated over a period of
50 years, would irnclude about 765,000 ma of saw timber, 2.2 million poles of
20 cm diameter, 1.75 million poles of 10 cm diameter, 2.6 million bags of
charcoal, and 85,600 Christmas trees. It could be assumed that without tha
type of support provided under the project, the Pine Forest, one of the last
contiguous forested areas of the country, would disappear and the investments
made under the previous pilot project would be lost. The value of this forest
far exceeds the relative low returns obtained from its expected timber
production, and includes the conservation of an important watershed, habitat
for fauna and flora, and touristic potential, which are not quantified.
6.03        The agroforestry extension services provided would induce some
16. 000 farmers in the project areas to apply on-farm soil conservation
measures, mainly use of grasses and hedgerow cultivation, plant timber and
fruit trees, and use improved seed of maize and beans. The recommended
combination of these measures would vary according to the location and the
steepness of the slopes. The meacures would generally aim 9* stabilizing and
increasing gross farm income without a significant increase  n farm costs
apart from the incremental labor needed to plant trees and establish
hedgerows, and the relatively small effect of harvesting additional products
and a heavier crop. The project would, as an incenitive to farmers, and
because farm credit is not available, support the farmer's efforts by the
supply of tree seedlings and hedgerow seed, either free of charge or at a
nominal cost and, for the first year of the farmer's participation in the
program, of improved maize and bean seed.
6.04        The benefits the farmer obtains from the agroforestry extension
program would consist oft (i) stemming the future decline in soil fertility;
(ii) improving the current levels of soil fertility; (iii) acquiring tree
products (firewood, poles, fruits); (iv) increasing the availability of fodder
for livestock; and (v) harvesting larger crops by using improved seed
varieties. For highland farms on flatter land (up to a 302 slope), where the
erosion impact is less and the hedgerow cultivation not required, farmers
would be cxpected to increase their net income, with labor input costed at the
market price of US$1/day, between 10 and 202 over the without project
situation. Farms on steeper slopes (302-60%) who participate in the extension
program would be expected to stabilize their declining net incomes. If the
proposed measures to stop soil erosion are not taken on these farms, their net
income would be expected to decline by some 302-50%. For farmers only



- 34 -
adopting tree planting, such as expected in the Belle Anse area, benefits
would be much lower.
6.05        The value of investing in extension services for farmers in these
critical areas, which are to be protected, by far exceeds the incremental
production expected from these farms, which is attractive to individual
farmers but marginal overall. The provision of free technical assistance is
important to halt the decline in their productivity and incom2e due to
environmental degradation, and relieve the pressure on them to seek
alternative income, including from indiscriminate exploitation and destruction
of common (State) land, and to move towards the cities. Support services are
also needed to make the farmers cooperate in the effective implementation of
the restrictive land use regulation3 and land leases to be introduced in
protected areas, and to use them as allies of the administration and buffer
against illegal intruders from the outside.
6.06        The household energy conservation subproject has an estimated ERR
of 57Z, and the improved circular charcoal stove ("BME-model") has a simple
payback time of 55 days, based on savings of 9.8 kg of charcoal per month. By
1997, the subproject would result in annual savings of nearly 17,000 m ton of
charcoal, or over 102 of projected consumption. This would be equivalent to
135,000 m3 of wood, or the sustained production from about 17,000 ha of
fuelwood plantations. Even though the project would not include large-scale
reforestation, because the technologies and institutional arrangements are not
yet available, the project's incremental forest production, both from State
managed forests and from the private farms, will increase the sustainable
woodfuel supply for subsistence and for sale. On the demand side, charcoal
consumption in urban areas would be reduced by 10% following the large-scale
introduction of energy-efficient stoves. In addition, the policy planning,
legal framework review, and taxation measures would contribute to move towards
economic pricing of woodfuels (charcoal is currently grossly underpriced
financially) and the generation of revenues for continued investment in
forestry and environmental protection.
6.07        The institutional strengthening aspects of the project would lead
to improvements of the overall framework for the development and protection of
forest resources and natural parks through policy and legal reforms, training
of professionals, research and pilot projects, and demarcation, inventories
and guard services for protected areas. It would integrate agroforestry
extension activities into the functions of the Government and provide a
framework by which agroforestry could be expanded as capacity grows and
additional external support is forthcoming in the context of the TFAP. The
development of institutions and technologies under the project would lay the
ground for more ambitious programs at a later stage, including for larger-
scale reforestation and water development programs. The costs of not
undertaking the protective measures proposed under the project would be
extremely high. Without the type of assistance to be provided under the
project, the chance to preserve and develop the two national parks in the high
mountains, and to identify and create additional forest and biosphere reserves
could be missed in view of the strong pressure for cutting of fuelvood, crop
production and grazing.



- 35 _
Environmental Impact
6.08        The overall environmental impact of tho project would be to:
(i) reduce the speed of land degradation, in particular the decline in soil
productivity, los of land suitable for agriculture and foresery, and water
retention; and (ii) maintain the habitat for unique forest ecosystems with
rare or endangered species of f:ora and fauna, and for human settl6ment;
(iii) generate additional production on a sustained basis (fuelvood, poles,
fruits, food crops and fodder); and (iv) reduce the demand for charcoal. This
would be achieved through the following actions and their impact:
(a) taking stock of existing natutral resources in Haiti through
inventories, surveys an.- the creation of a data base. This would
provide a basis for settirg priorities and defining future policy
adjustments and investments;
(b) setting aside and restricting access to protect and manage forest
ecosystems and natural parks. This would tend to stem further
destruction of these areas by indiscriminating tree cutting,
grazing and agricultural uses
(c) zoning of land use in protected areas (biosphere concept). This
would improve land use by defining the optimal use of these
protected areas in balance between the interests of preservation of
ecosystems and the sustainable utilization of the resource for
forestry, agriculture, grazing and, potentially, tourism;
(d) implementing conservation measures, e.g., in the core zones of the
biosphere reserves (absolute protection), and on special sites to
preserve the gene stock and for multiplication. This would allow
the preservation of vital ecosystems, and rare and endangered plant
and animal species; and
(e)   regulating private land use in protected areas through:
(i) Government provided protective services (forest and park
rangers) for law enforcement; (ii) a land lease system which would
enhance land tenure security and private investment in conservation
measures, while providing a contractual relationship that would
allow the application of zoning regulations, and some cost
recovery; and (iii) the provision of agricultural support services
(exteriion, input supply, small civil works for water supply) that
would ;romote soil and water conservation, farm forestry, use of
improved seeds, and foster farmer organizations. The combined
impact of these measures would be to increase investment,
production and income on a sustained basis for the benefit of the
population already settled in these protected areas.
6.09       In addition, the project would improve soil productivity and the
cultivation of existing traditional crops by conservation measures and tree
planting, and not through the use of agrochemicals. Aside from some thinning
operations in the Pine Forest to enhance growth of the existing stands, no
logging would be undertaken during the implementation period of the project.



- 36 -
Existing roads in the Pine Forest would be maintained. Road access to the
La Visite Park would be improved, but only through an indirect route from the
South, which because of its distance from Port-au-Prince would not attract
visitors.
Risks
6.10       The main risks of the project relate tos  (i) uncertain political
stability which may impact on the management and functioning of the
implementing agencies; (ii) institutional weaknesses of Government agencies
and Government commitment to streamline the operations of DRN and BME and
implement the policy agenda. To minimize the risks, the project's objectives
and scale of operations have been kept in line with the institutional reality,
in spite of the unquestionable need to progress more rapidly; conditionality
for employment of senior staff in the implementing agencies would be
introduced; payment from the credit for incremental salaries and allowances
would be subject to ainual ceilings, and permanently required staff would be
gradually transferred from credit to Government funding to ensure
sustainability; technical assistance would be hired where local expertise is
not available; to alleviate the Government's management and supervision
.equirements, an international firm of consultants would be employed to
provide the technical assistance and virtually all consultants; contrectors
would be used wherever possible to avoid financially not sustainable build-up
of bureaucratic structures; work programs and budgets would be annually agreed
with IDA; and a mid-term review of the performance of the project would be
used to redesign the project as required.
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
7.01        During Negotiations of the proposed credit the following
assuranaces were obtained from the Government regarding the content of major
policy actions of the Government's program and their implementation under the
project:
(a)   Regulatory Framework.  The Government would submit by March 309
1993 for IDA's comments a proposal for changes to its natural
resource legislation aimed inter alia at increasir.g the incentives
for farmers to plant trees by reducing the existing restrictions
on harvesting and use, making the legislation consistent with the
objectives of the Government's development strategy, and
strengthening the institutional capabilities for enforcing such
legislation. Assurances were also obtained that all measuras,
including submission of draft bills to Congress if needed, which
are required for the implementation of the changes, would be taken
no later than December 31, 1993 (para. 4.05 (c)); and
(b)   Forestry Domain.  The Government would, following the completion
of the necessary surveys, take all measures, including submission
of legislation to Congress, if needed, to ensure MARNDR's control
and management of the following State lands for natural resource
protection and development: (i) the existing Pine Forest Reserve,



- 37 -
and the existing La Visite and Pic Macaya National Parks by
September 309 1992 (paras. 4.08 (a) and 4.10 (b))g (ii) additional
forest reserves totalling 30,000 ha by September 30, 1994
(paras 4.08 (b)); and (iii) one additional national park by
September 309 1994 (para. 4.10 (a)).
7.02        Puring Negottations of the proposed credit the following other
agreements and assurances were obtained from the Government as part of the
project's Implementation Program:
(a)   Woodfuels Taxation.  The Government would submit by
September 30, 1992 for IDA's comments a proposal for changes to
its woodfuel taxation system aimed inter alia at improving the
collection, reporting and accounting of the woodfuel taxes,
improving the enforcement capabilities in the application of the
woodfuel tax regulations, and setting tax rates at the appropriate
levels. It was also agreed that all measures, including
submission of legislation to Congress, if needed, which are
required for the implementation of the changes would be taken no
later than December 31, 1993 (para. 4.05 (d));
(b)   Staffing.  The following positions would be staffed and maintained

HAITI
FORESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
Regulatorv Framework for Natural Resources. Forest Reserves and Parks
Present Situation
1.          Jurisdictions.  According to statute, the Ministry of Agriculture,
Natural Resources and Rural Development (MARNDR) has primary responsibility
for management of national parks and forest lands. Within the agricultural
ministry, the Directorate of Natural Resources (DRN) is charged with
responsibility for national forest reserves and natural parks. The DRN houses
the following natural resource and environmental services:
Forestry Resources Service (SRF)
Environmental Protection Service (SPE)
Technical Secretariat for Watershed Management (STABV)
Watershed Management Service (SABV)
Service of Aquaculture and Maritime Fishing
Water Resources Service (SRE)
Irrigation and Rural Engineering Service (SIG)
2.          The Forest Resources Service (SRF) manages forest reserves and
parks. It is also charged with collection of wood related taxes,
authorizatica of tree cutting on private lands and management of the Special
Reforestation Fund. The Envirormental Protection Service (SPE) is charged
with parks management and natural resource inventories; however, it does not
have staff or program funds to carry out these functions. Two semi-autonomous
Government agencies also have responsibility for parks:
Institute for the Safeguard of the National Patrimony (ISPAN)
National Office of Tourism and Public Relations (ONTRP)
ISPAN has served as a channel for USAID funding to two natural parks, La
Visite and Pic Macaya. In 1988 ISPAN relinquished this funding and
transferred its responsibility to the SRF. This transfer took place through
an exchange of letters between ISPAN and the SRF. ISPAN retains primary
responsibility for national historic parks and monuments, including the
Citadelle fortress. ONTRP does not actively manage natural parks nor any
other protected areas. MARNDR is the only Government agency with active
responsibility for national natural parks.



-57-
ANNEX 2
Page 2 of 12
3.           Parks and Reserves.  Three major natural sites have been set
aside by law as protected areass the Pine Forest Reserve (27,871 ha), Morne
La Visite National Park (3,000 ha), and Pic Macaya National Park (5,500 ha).
The Pine Forest Reserve is managed by the SRF as a forestry production site.
Two other high mountain sites, Pic Macaya and Morne La Visite, were granted
the status of "national natural parks" by the Decree of June 18, 1983.  The
two mountain parks are under the jurisdiction of the agricultural ministry and
assigned to the forest service. The 1983 law also named eight other "national
parks" and "natural sites" including three springs, four historic monuments
and Lake Peligre. With the exception of the Citadelle and Lake Peligre, these
sites are composed of only a few hectares each. Historic monuments and their
restoration are under the management of ISPAN. Lake Peligre and other water
parks are not actively managed as protected areas.
4.          The SRI provides services in the Pine Forest and the two mountain
parks. The SRI initiated direct management of the Pine Forest Reserve under
the Pilot Forestry Project (1982-1989). The SRF carries out classic forest
management functions and employs civilian and military guards to protect the
forest. This level of public management on state land is without precedent in
Haiti, but it is not yet sufficient to prevent agricultural encroachment and
illicit harvest. The SRF also maintains civilian guards in Morne La Visite
and Pic Macaya Hatioral Parks, but it is unable to field a parks management
team and other technical services. In Pic Macaya Park, the University of
Florida operates a non-governmental project emphasizing park protection and
extension services to local farmers. Horne La Visite Park is the most
vulnerable of the three sites due to the absence of both technical staff and
site-based supervision of park guards.
s.          Legal Framework and Enforcement.  Since the 1920s there have been
numerous laws which reinforce the Ministry of Agriculture and strengthen legal
protection of forests and other natural resources. Current forest law is
articulated in the Law of August 17, 1955, which regulates cutting, transport
and commerce in wood, and the Rural Code of 1962. The Rural Code serves as
the primary regulatory framework for rural Haiti. The Decree of
November 20, 1972, created the Special Reforestation Fund which serves as the
legal basis for the forest service to collect taxes on wood products, levy
fines and confiscate illicit wood products. The Constitution of 1987 provides
for protection of the national patrimony including the natural environment,
natural sites, flora and fauna, and the ecological balance. The Decree of
July 7, 1987, establishes current guidelines for enforcement. This decree
prohibits tree cutting and transport of wood products except by permission of
the Ministry of Agriculture. Cutting authorization is to be sought from an
agent of the ministry. These civilian agents are empowered to collect taxes,
arrest violators and confiscate illegal wood products.
6.          The law has ample provisions to create protected areas.  There is
a longstanding body of law which provides for the creation of "reserved zones"
and "zones under protection". Zones under protection are defined as sites
where use is regulated by the agricultural ministry, and reserved zones are



-58-
ANNEX 2
Page 3 of 12
defined as sites withdrawn from all forms of production. The Rural Code of
1962 provides that all state forest reserves are to be administered by the
agricultural ministry. Laws and decrees on national parks also assign land
management responsibility to the agricultural ministry.
7.          In terms of enforcement, the agricultural ministry supervises a
corps of 60 Forestry Agents charged with collection of wood transport taxes
and authorization of tree cutting on private lands. The Forestry Agents are
attached to MARNDR agricultural districts dispersed throughout the country.
Another 30 Forestry Agents are known as Wood Products Controllers. These
agents are directly supervised by the SRF. They are responsible for verifying
payment of wood transport taxes at checkpoints along major roadways and ports
entering Port-au-Prince. The tax collection rate for wood transport taxes is
estimated at about 102 of total volume. The SRF also hires Park Agents for
enforcement of tree cutting and agricultural regulations in Park Pic Macaya
and Park Morne La Visite. These civilian park agents are not trained and they
are poorly supervised. The SRF has a corps of civilian forestry guards in
both units of the Pine Forest Reserve. Park agents and forestry guards have
tended to fluctuate in number. Their recruitment and salary payment have been
dependent on external sources of funding. Since February 1988 the Haitian
Armed Forces have seconded a corporal and two soldiers to the Pine Forest
Reserve under direct MARNDR command for assistance in enforcement activities
in the forest. The army also assigned an army major as liaison officer for
the SRF. The SRF pays expenses and transport costs for the seconded army
personnel and liaison officer.
8.          Land Tenure.  Haiti has important forest resources in both public
and private sectors. The state owns all offshore islands. The state also has
large holdings in high mountain zones, plateau areas, barren savannas and arid
coastal plains. The full extent of state holdings is unknown. Relatively
little state land has been surveyed and demarcated.
9.          State lands are held under distinctly contrasting forms of public
management. The state uses two basic systems of classification: (a) public
domain of the state; and (b) private domain of the state. The Ministry of
Finance is charged with management of the private domain of the state,
including land leasing to small farmers and to holders of large corporate
concessions. Other ministries are assigned management of public domain lands.
The Ministry of Agriculture is charged with management of forest reserves and
natural park preserves as public domain of the state.
10.         In some areas of Haiti there are large amounts of unassigned
(unleased) state lands in both the public and private domains of the state.
Such unoccupied state lands are open to all, and wood resources are simply
mined. Most of the unassigned state lands fall within the private domain of
the state managed by the Ministry of Finance. In areas of dry forest,
unassigned lands are commonly used for open range grazing. In general,
unassigned lands are subject to illegal and uncontrolled usage for
agriculture, grazing and harvest of forest products. Unassigned lands are
particularly subject to uncontrolled fires.



-59-
6NNE 2
Page 4 of 12
II.         Haitian land tenure is a private property system based on a dual
system of arrangements--customary usage as well as the formal legal framework.
A considerable proportion of the land is held without the benefit of cadastre
and updated title. Most private agricultural land in Haiti is owned or
occupied by small peasant farmers--especially in the mountainous zones which
constitute the vast majority of the nation's territory. Peasant farm units
are generally composed of several small dispersed plots. Peasant farmland is
held through a pattern of mixed tenure whereby farmers aimultaneously rent,
sharecrop, own and lease out plots to other peasant farmers. Land in low-
lying plains--about one-fourth of the national territory--is held by a mix of
large and small holders, but large holdings tend to predominate. Large
holdings are generally controlled by absentee landlords. Many large holdings
are subdivided into small parcels farmed by sharecroppers.
12.         Land Leases on State Land.  Land falling within the public domain
may be leased out, but such lands are inalienable--unlike the private domain
of the state which may be leased, sold, donated or homesteaded. The Decree of
July 27, 1989, establishes leaseholders in the private domain of the state as
users rather than renters. It establishes a nine year renewable contract
rather than the annual renewable lease which has otherwise been standard
practice in the private domain of the state. This decree has the force of law
but the Ministry of Finance has not taken concrete steps tc implement the new
law. Many state leaseholders in the private domain of the state have ceased
to pay annual rents since the 1989 law was publicized. In general, there has
been a significant decrease in state rental collections since 1986.
13.         The Pine Forest Reserve is managed by the Forest Service as public
domain. Pine Forest leaseholders continue to pay annual rents. Longstanding
leaseholders in Morne La Visite Park have not beer required to pay rents since
1983 when MARNDR announced a policy of eviction on park lands slated for
preservation. Evicted leaseholders were offered access to farmland on other
sites as compensation. This policy has not been fully implemented, and old
leaseholders still maintain agricultural and grazing activities in the park.
In Pic Macaya Park the Ministry of Finance has ceased to collect rents. The
Ministry of Agriculture has evicted farmers from some areas, but it has not
introduced new leasing policies. The Ministry of Agriculture has the
authority to implement site management plans, multiple year lease contracts
and variable conditions for retaining leases. It also has the authority to
rescind leases and establish specialized land use zones and restricted areas.
The ministry has a broad base of authority, but it lacks the means to
establish a proper parks management system and to implement new leasing
policies on park lands.
Constraints
14.         Ecoloaical Crisis.  (a) Haiti's natural resource base is heavily
stressed. Wood based energy supplies are diminishing. Natural forest has
nearly disappeared, and rare species of flora and fauna are on the verge of
extinction. The scope and extreme urgency of the situation make it difficult



-60-
ANNHIL&
Page 5 of 12
to intervene effectively; (b) long-range policies of natural resource
conservation compete with urgent human needs for food, fuel and agricultural
livelihoodsl and (a) national philosophies of nature preservation and
environmental concern are at an embryonic stage of development.
15.         Natural Resource Information and fonitorina.  Despite the extreme
gravity of its ecological problems, Haiti has never undertaken a thoroub.i
invento.y of forestry resources and related ecosystems:  (a) there is only an
incomplete understanding of the location and magnitude of Haitian forests;
(b) the borders of most state lands are not delineated and marked. The full
extent of state ownership of forest resources is unknown; and (c) there is no
Government agency with the institutional capacity to monitor Haiti's natural
ecosystems and genetic stock.
16.         Jurisdictions.  There are jurisdictional problems in managing
state lands: (a) important forest ecosystems exist on land controlled by the
Ministry of Finance as private domain of the state. These forest ecosystems
are unmanaged and unprotected; and (b) the Pine Forest Reserve and the two
major natural parks are not served by civil and military jurisdictions which
correspond with the borders of these protected areas. Law enforcement in each
forest reserve and park is assured by a multiplicity of local jurisdictions -
military posts, rural sections and court jurisdictions under the authority of
officials based in distant municipalities.
17.         Legislation and Enforcement.  The body of law governing forestry
and natural resources is characterized by the following problems: (a) the law
is poorly enforced. The Government does not have the institutional means to
implement forestry and natural resource law; (b) the law is contradictory and
poorly adapted to the socioeconomic context of rural Haiti. It is to a
considerahle extent unenforceable; (c) the law emphasizes prohibitions with
little attention to incentives. The law does not adequately protect the tree
harvest rights of peasant tree planters; (d) these characteristics of the law
promote non-compliance and serve as disincentives to protection of trees and
forests; (e) forestry agents charged vith tax collection on wood products are
poorly trained and inadequately supervised. The Special Reforestation Fund is
non-fiscalized and administered without the benefit of modern tools of
information management, accounting, budgeting and program planning; (f) there
is a general lack of knowledge of the larw among the citizenry as well as local
authorities responsible for law enforcement; and (g) natural resource law is
not linked to a clear national policy on forestry and natural resources.
18.         Land Tenure.  There are important land tenure constraints to
managing forestry and natural resources in Haiti:  (a) on private lands,
farmers are unwilling to plant trees on plots where they cannot be assured of
harvest control over the trees. Rational management of natural resources is
limited by the fragmented character of peasant landholding, absentee
landholding and subleasing. Most peasant farmers do not have access to
agricultural extension and agroforestry extension services; (b) important
forest ecosystems on state lands remain outside of the formal protective
framework offered by the option of reclassification into the public domain as



-61-
&uIX 2
Page 6 of 12
forest reserves and park preserves; and (a) national parks and forest reserves
are subject to agricultural use, grazing and wood cutting by local residents
and non-residents.
19.         Lease k_anaaement.  Leasing practices on state lands serve as a
constraint to rational land management: (a) leases are generally written as
annual contracts without assurance of long-term access; (b) leased plots are
unverified by site visits or survey; (c) management of state leases is not
supported by modern information management systems and procedural
accountability; (d) the practice of absentee lease-holding and subleasing
interferes with rational land use on state lands; (e) rental collection on
state lands is inconsistent; (f) unassigned land is characterized by
destructive land use practices based on unregulated free access; and (g) the
MARNDR eviction policy in natural parks has not been fully Implemented.
20.         Institutional Constraints.  Institutional weaknesses are the key
limiting factor in protecting Haiti's natural resources: (a) state lands are
essentially unmanaged. Formal responsibility for public lands is not matched
by the institutional capability to implement programs; (b) Haiti has important
human resources, but there are significant gaps in training. Few Haitians are
trained in ecology, wildlife conservation, natural resource planning, forest
management, land use monitoring or remote sensing; (c) the formal creation of
forest reserves and park preserves is a very recent development. There is no
tradition of rational land management on state lands; (d) technical services
of the Government have limited reach. This has fostered the emergence of non-
governmental alternatives and parallel systems. Government has little
experience in collaborating with non-governmental organizations or
coordinating joint public-private efforts. The Natural Resource Department
has little direct experience with extension, or supervision of contracts for
extension services; and (e) there is little Government funding to carry out
programs in forestry and natural resource conservation. The DRN is poorly
equipped and unable to make optimal use of its trained people. This
perpetuates a lack of program continuity and inhibits the development of
sustainable institutions for natural resource management.
21.         Persnectives.  The ecological crisis in Haiti argues strongly in
favor of investments targeted at protection and management of the country's
dwindling natural resources. The problem of competing short-range and long-
term needs can be addressed by incorporating the needs of local populations
into the overall system for regulating and managing key natural resources. It
is feasible to assist the Government of Haiti in formulating natural resource
policies, strengthening local institutions devoted to natural resource
management and identifying priorities for intervention. It is feasible to
carry out a national inventory of forestry resources and create a mechanism
for monitoring land use.
22.         Jurisdictional constraints cannot be fully addressed.
Jurisdictional problems can be improved by transferring management control of
priority sites to the Ministry of Agriculture, and by creating a coherent
approach to regulation and management of state lands. The law on forestry and



-62-
ANESt 2
Page 7 of 12
natural resource protectiun could be Improved by linking it to national policy
commitments, and recommending revisions to law based on pragmatic analysis of
the social and institutional context of law enforcement. The Special
Reforestation Vund could be improved as a tool for implementing forest policy,
and generating revenues to cover recurring costs of forest and parks
management.
23.         Land tenure constraints to rational forest and natural resource
management cannot be fully r3solved. It is neither feasible nor desirable to
underwrite the cost of a national cadastre at this time. It is possible to
create enhanced incentives for peasants to plant trees through the instruments
of taxation, lease management on state lands and targeted extension services.
It is feasible to improve the management of selected public lands through land
use planning, zoning, boundary surveys, visible demarcation and law
enforcement.
24.         In terms of the institutional constraints, it is not feasible to
underwrite the cost of strengthening the entire Ministry of Agriculture nor
even its Department of Natural Resources at this time. It is feasible to
enhance the DRN's ability to regulate and manage national parks and forest
reserves, and to develop a long-range strategy for rational management of the
country's natural resources.
Project Comionents Relatinx to the Reaulatorv Framework
25.         In terms of overall strategy, the project would make use of the
concept of Biosphere Reserves as a guiding motif for natural resource
management. This would provide for multipla land use strategies including
production goals as well as preservation. The project would seek to establish
balanced relationships between people and natural ecosystems; designate land
use zones for specified uses such as forestry, preservation of biodiversity,
agriculture, agroforestry and controlled grazing, and take the needs of local
agrarian population into account while enforcing forestry laws and zoning
restrictions.
26.         Lesal Services.  Legal counsel to the project would be provided
for review of project related contracts, and legal representation of the
MARNDR in matters pertaining to land tenure, enforcement of forest legislation
and the establishment of national forest reserves and parks. Two MARNDR
lawyers would be retained for these functions. Project support for DRN would
also include three short-term consultancies (two months) for social and legal
review of the law on forestry and natural resources, and of the prevailing
system of law enforcement (see Appendix to Annex 2 for consultant terms of
reference).
27.         Legal and Social Review of Legislation and Law Enforcement.  This
review would generate recommendations for revisions in the law, and
recommendations regarding the institutional requirements for implementing the
law and carrying out law enforcement. The social and legal review would be
guided by Haitian governmental policy on forestry and natural resources. The



-63-
Page 8 of 12
review would also be informed by needs and concerns raised by the DRN Director
and the staff of the SRP and the SPE. The recommendations of the legal and
social review would serve as a basis for the DRN Director to draft new
legislation for enactment through the legislative process or presidential
decree. The Minister of Agriculture would present draft legislation to the
Council of Ministers and actively promote its enactment into law. The legal
and social review would have been completed by the second year of the project.
The enactment of amendments, new provisions and repeals to law would have been
accomplished by the midpoint of the 5-year project (30 months). Enactment of
revisions to law would be followed by implementation of the law by Government
authorities and institutions during the third year of the project.
28.         Social and legal review of forcestry and natural resource
legislation would be undertaken by a team of four specialists (see Appendix to
Annex 2) including two internationally recruited consultants--a legal
specialist in forestry and environmental legislation, and a social scientist
with experience in forestry, natural resource management and peasant farm
systems; and two Haitian specialists including an SRP staff forester or
agronomist familiar with peasant agricultural systems, forestry and natural
resource issues in Haiti, and a local lawyer with experience in Haitian land
tenure issues, forestry and natural resource legislation, law enforcement, and
legal proceedings in rural Haiti including the coexistence of law end
customary practice.
29.         Transfer of State Land.  The DRN Director would oversee the
process of transferring jurisdiction over state lands targeted for MARNDR
protection and management as public domain of the state. State lands would be
transferred out of the private domain of the state managed by the tax office,
the Direction G6n6rale des Impots (DGI) of the Ministry of Finance.
30.         The procedure for reclassification of state lands would require
the following steps: upon approval of recommendations made by the DRN
Director, the Minister of Agriculture would initiate the transfer of state
land into public domain assigned to MARNDR. The Minister would prepare a
draft decree for consideration and approval by the Council of Ministers. Upon
approval the decree would be signed into law by the president and published in
Le Moniteur, the official organ of the Government. The decree would identify
the site or sites to be transferrQd including location, purpose of transfer
and size (area). The Ministry of Agriculture would be named as the authority
aseuming responIIbiiity for the site under the rule. o; public domaiua.  The
Ministry of Agriculture would assign site management redponsibility to the DRN
and its constituent services, the SRP or the SPE.

32.         Lease Administration.  Reclassification of state land would
inclv.de transfer of responsibility for land leases held under land contracts
with the Ministr"v of Finance a: the time of transfer.  The Ministry of Finance
would relinquish any further Tesponsibility for land leasing on these public
domain sites. The SRP and SPE would establish land leasing policies in forest
reserves and parks as prescribed by site management plans and zoning
restrictions established for each park and preserve. Lease administration
would be used as a tool for rational land management of parks and preserves.
33.         The project would carry out a census of farmers and farm plots at
each park and reserve. In areas zoned for agricultural use, new lease
contracts would be established for 10 year periods to provide incentives for
rational land management and conservation farming. Old lease contracta in
areas zoned out of agricultural use would be terminated in keeping with MARNDR
policy for preservation of natural parks. Evicted farmers able to show bona
fide lease contracts, and evidence of direct continuous occupancy for at leaot
five years, would be offered alternative agricultural sites within the park or
reserve, or the financial equivalent (one year's rent). The SRF land rent is
25 gourdes per carreaul pa: year in the Pine Forest Reserve.
34.         Long-term leases would be renewed on an annual basis upon payment
of the lease price. Contract renewal would be conditional upon adherence to
conservation practices prescribed in farm plans written for each documented
plot. Implementation of farm plans would be supported by extension services
provided by NGO agroforestry extension contractors. Farm plans T'rould be
jointly developed by the farmer and the NGO, and approved by the SRF or the
SPE. Implementation of farm plans would be verified by site visits. Park and
forest rangers would assist the SPE/SRF in collecting annual rents. SRF and
SPE receipts from leasing would be fiscalized through the Special
Reforestation Fund.
35.         SPE and SRE  leasing policies would require the leaseholder to
reside within the park or reserve and work the land directly. There would be
no subleasing arravgements or absentee leaseholders. Annual lease renewal
would be contingent upon maintenance of approved garden perimeters (no
expansion of existing garden borders, no new establishment of unregulated

garden plots), suspension of the use of fire for land clearing, use of

36.         Law Enforcement Services.  Enforcement of forestry laws and land
use regue.ations would be assured by park rangers in national parks under SPE
management, forest rangers in forest reserves und r SRI management, a small
corps of military forestry guards assigned to the Pine Forest Reserve, and
forestry agents charged with collection of forestry taxes in the 22
Agricultural Districts of the Ministry of Agriculture.
37.         The project would create a civilian corps of 30 park rangers under
the SPE, and 60 forest rangers under the SRE. The park rangers and forest
rangers would have similar core training. They would receive additional on-
the-job training adapted to the special needs of parks management and forest
reserva management in accord with their specific assignments. In general, the
ranger corps would be a key element in the overall management of national
parks mnd reserves. They would assist in land lease administration,
implementation of special land use zones, protection of borders, enforcement
of forestry laws and land use restrictions, arrest of violators, confiscation
of illegal wood products and collection cf fines and wood transport taxes.
38.         Each park and forest reserve would have a Ranger Unit directly
employed and supervised by the SPE or the SRF. Rangers would be under the
immediate supervision of ranger supervisors for each Ranger Unit. Ranger
Units and Ranger Supervisors would be under the authority of SPE and SRF
professional personnel (agronomists or foresters) assigned as site managers
for each park and forest reserve. Ranger services in the Pine Forest Reserve
would build upon the existing corps of civilian guards. The Pine Forest would
require two Ranger Units, one for Unit 1 (20 rangers and a unit leader), and
the other for Unit 2 (10 rangers and a unit leader). Both Pine Forest Ranger
Units would be under the overall supervision of the Unit 1 ranger supervisor.
Ranger logistics and radio-communications would allow close supervision,
regular patrols and rapid deployment of rangers to deal with violations,
arrests and confiscation of illicit wood products.
39.         The basic law enforcement orientation of rangers would be to
stress prevention. They would disseminate information to the local population
in matters of the law, and park and reserve regulations. They would be
instructed in due process for carrying out arrests, levying fines and wood
taxes, and confiscation of illicit wood products. They would be required to
seek formal warrants from the local Peace Court (Tribunal de Paix) for the
arrest of violators. In the case of arrests en flagrant deli, they would
prepare a formal report (process-verbal) and turn violators over to army
personnel until the case could be heard in the Peace Court. Rangers would be
prepared to participate in court hearings when alleged violators are judged.

40.         Rangers would be carefully screened and selected on the basis of

identification cardo, radio communications and horses. Rangers would be
assigned to specified zones, and checkpoints along major roadways of parks and
reserves. There would be no solitary assignments without access to other
rangers or supervisors. Head rangers would make frequent supervisory visits
to all rangers under their command.

43.         EMAF would train park and forest rangers, seconded army personnel
and forestry agents (collectors of wood taxes) in two-week orientation
sessions given by EMAP at the onset of employment, and further in-service
training consisting of annual three-day seminars (see Annex 7 for training).
Training content would include knowledge of the law, due process, procedures
for arrest, procedures for confiscation of illicit material, rules of
evidence, citizen's rights and forestry and natural resource legislation.
Forest and park rangers would be trained in the basic principles of

and basic technical matters such as fire control and management of natural
regeneration. As site plans are developed, rangers would be carefully trained
in the goals and content of site management plan and special land use zones.

FORESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
Pine Forest  -aaament,
I. Present Situation
1.         'The Pine Forest Reserve Is located approximately 100 km southeast
of Port-au-Prince along the crest of the La Selle Massif. Natural Pinus
occidentalls forests originally covered much of the mountainous and central
plateau areas of Haiti. A FAO inventory in 1977 found a total of 38,646 ha
remaining in the La Selle area. Bota the standing volume and the surface area
forested are steadily diminishing as the pine stands are regularly threatened
by clearing for agricultural purposes, illegal log and resinous fatwood
harvesting, and wildfires. The vildfires are especially abusive as they
eliminate the often profuse natural regeneration and expose the pole-sized
stems to unregulated harvesting. Approximately 6,500 peasants live within the
Reserve and actively cultivate approximately 1,500 agricultural parcels
totaling 8,560 ha. Because of the insecure nature of their tenure, and the
prevailing official treatment of peasants as obstacles to forest management; a
tenuous and often antagonistic relationship exists between Government
officials and local populations.
2.          The "Projet Porestier National" (PPN) initiated Pine Forest
management In 1983. The work of the PPM consisted of: the delimitation and
marking of the Reserve, an inventory of 11,400 ha, studies of growth and
volume, the preparation of a general Forest Management Plan for 1990-93,
reforestation of 320 ha with various trial species, a census of 500 farmers
within the Reserve and regulation of 300 land leases for 400 ha, and the
preparation of forest type, potential erosion and land utilization maps. The
forest was found to be very heterogenous; basal area varying from 0 to 30 mt
per hectare and annual growth from 2 to 9 mh per hectare per year. Following
the Reserve delimitation, the total Reserve area was found to be 27,871 ha.
The PFN had a traditional "western" forest management orientation and did not
adequately adapt forest management strategy to include the needs and concerns
of local populations.
II. Pronosed Obiectives and Strateev
Obiectives
3.    The objectives of the Pine Forest Management component are to:
(a)   ensure the security and integrity of the remaining pine forest
stands by protecting the forest from fire, agricultural
encroachment, and illegal harvesting;



-74-
ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 7
(b)   complete the collection of basic forest management information
(e.g., forest inventory, stand exams and maps, and growth and
yield tables);
(c)   prepare a comprehensive, multiple-use oriented, Pine Forest
Management Plan;
(d)   establish the SRF's capacity to regulate and manage the Pine
Forest Reserve for sustained yield of wood products and the
protection of biological diversity;
(e)   complete the census of farmers inhabiting the Reserve, survey
their land holdings and regulate their land leases; and
(f)   establish the Pine Forest Reserve as a permanent entity vhich
vould be professionally managed by the SRF and include the local
population as integral and productive parAcipants in the
management, protection and benefits of the Reserve.
Strateag
4.    Ca)   The SRP would complete the work begun by the PFN in inventory,
farmer census, agricultural land regulation, and reforestation,
and initiate new efforts in the protection of natural
regeneration, thinning, forest protection and fire control. The
purpose of this second phase of Pine Forest Management is to
prepare both the forest and the Forest Service for full forest
management and commercial harvesting. The commercial harvesting
of saw logs (which is scheduled for eight years after project
initiation) would not be authorized until the above objectives of
this project have been fully met; and
Cb)   A NGO would be contracted by the Project Director to provide
comprehensive agroforestry services to the peasant population
within the Reserve and within the surrounding buffer zone (as per
Annex 5). These support services would be engaged in conjunction
with the SRI farmer census and lease regulation activities. SRI
staff would supervise and periodically evaluate the field work of
the NGO.
III. Proiect Components
Forest Inventory and Manatement Plan
5.          Inventors. Stand Examination and Zonint.  Of the approximately
16,000 forested hectares within the Pine Forest, 11,400 have been inventoried
to date. The SRP would complete the inventory on the remaining forested areas
and subsequently refine the volume and growth tables. Upon completion of the
forest inventory, the technical team would begin the examination and mapping
of all ecologically and geographically distinct forest stands vithin the
Reserve. Information including location, age, history, edaphic site
conditions, endemic flora and fauna would be collected for each stand. This



-75-                        M 3
Page 3 of 7
information would be used in the preparation of the forest management plan.
The Pine Foret Management team would also collaborate with specialists from
the Environmental Protection Service (SPE) to identify and delineate
conservation zones within the Pine Forest Reserve where wood production would
be excluded. Such conservation zones would include those reserved for the
protection of threatened flora and fauna, unique habitats, and fragile lands.
6.          Forest Managgament Plan.  The 1990-93 Forest Management Plan
prepared by the Senior Forestry Consultant would be enhanced and expanded with
the completion of the forest inventory, stand examination and conservation
zone identification. In addition to the description of overall forest
management and administration components, this Plan would include:
(a) forest stand-type specific management plans and maps;
(b)   land use plans and maps for the conservation zones identified with
the SPE; and
(c)   a complete description of the organizational arrangements planned
for the administration of commercial harvesting contracts.
Farmer Census and Boundary Survey
7.          Census and Land Leases.   The Project would complete the farmer
census, inventory and survey of the approximately 1,500 agricultural parcels
within Units 1 and 2 of the Reserve. This work would conclude with new land
lease contracts with each farmer concerned. New lease length would vary from
5 to 10 years and the leases would be renewed on an annual basis upon payment
of the lease price. Contract renewal would be conditional upon the
Implementation of conservationist treatments as described in a parcel specific
farm plan. Lease contracts could be canceled at any time the contract
specifications are breached. Farm plans would be jointly developed by the
farmer and the NGO with authorization from the SRF. The character and phasing
of these treatments would be mutually agreed upon by the SRF and the NGO
implementing the agroforestry extension sub-project. All annual cropping
agricultural activity on land deemed inappropriate by the SRF (e.g., lands
with slopes greater than 602) would be suspended. This land would then be
reforested at the expense of the SRF.
8.          Boundary Survey.  The SRP would survey and monument the boundaries
of agricultural lands within the perimeter of the Pine Forest Reserve. The
approximately 200 km of boundary between leased agricultural land and Forest
Reserve would be marked regularly with posts grounded in cement and metallic
signs on trees. The SRF would also refine the Forest Reserve pe-imeter survey
conducted by the Dlrectlon Gdn6rale des Impots (DGI) in 1989. A survey team
would be contracted to place boundary markers approximately every 100 metors
between the reference points established by DGI. This work would clarify the
irregular boundary and clearly establish property lines. A crew of locally
knowledgeable SRP personnel would work alongside the contract survey crew to
ensure the recognition of the existing property boundaries.



-76-                       ANNEX 3
Page 4 of 7
Reforestation
9.          An estimated 1,500 ha of land with slopes greater that 60Z, and
other cleared land would be reforested. The taungya reforestation system is
proposed of cleared or under-stocked stands which are currently utilized for
agricultural purposes. Upon the achievement of adequate stocking, the SRF
would provide the farmer with another parcel of under-stocked forest. The
principal species utilized would be Pinus occidentalis. Nursery stock would
supplement the stock of widlings collect6d on site. The tree nursery located
in Grenier (75 km from the Reserve) would be transferred to the Pine Forest
Reserve area. Annual nursery production would average 350,000 seedlings.
Protection of Natural Regeneration
10.         Pinus occidentalis is a prolific seeder and the natural
regeneration is vigorous. Very susceptible to wildfire damage from ages 0 to
15, an estimated 2,100 ha of natural regeneration in this age class require
protection. The project would identify all sites of substantial natural
regeneration and protect them with a 3 meter wide fire-break. These fire-
breaks would be maintained until the stand reached the age of 15 and each
stand would be patrolled regularly during the fire season.
Fire Control
11.         The project would establish a fire control unit which would enable
rapid fire detection, and successful intervention. This service would be
facilitated by the use of FM portable radios, and coordinated by a fire
control leader stationed at the SRF office in the "Foret des Pins" village.
Watchtowers would be constructed on La Selle, Mare Rouge and Vincent mountains
and manned with firemen and radios during the fire season. Volunteer fire
brigades would be established in each of the ten villages within the Reserve.
A radio operator would be stationed full-time at the SRF office.
Forest Protection and Other Revenues
12.         Thinnine.  Natural stands would be thinned to the levels
recommended in the 1990-93 Forest Management Plan (approximately 250 trees
per ha by stand age 30). It is estimated that for the next 15 years it would
be desirable to cut stems/ha on 100 ha/year. This estimate will be refined
following the completed forest inventory.
13.         Christmas Tree Production.  An incipient and illicit Christmas
tree market already exists in the Pine Forest. An allowable harvest of
200 trees per year for the next eight years is estimated. When the
reforestation plantations reach age eight, two hectares per year will be
harvested (producing 2,000 trees per year).
14.         Tourism.  Tourism in the Pine Forest is becoming more popular and
weekend visitors frequently rent one of the seven bungalows available for
tourists. The cost per night varies between US$10 and US$15 dependent upon
the bungalow. 500 person-nights are expected annually.



-77-                    AE
Page 5 of 7
15.         Land Rents.  The official land rent is US$5.0/carreauxlyear for
cultivated land within the Pine Forest Reserve. The total number of
cultivated carreaux within t:..a Reserve is 8,560, which indicates that total
receipts could equal US$40,000 per yea.. A fraction of this amount ts
currently collected. The project would reinforce the rent collection system,
using the new, long-term land leases and the subsequent guaranteed land
security as incentive for payment. The receipts would be fiecalized by the
SRP administ"ator and would be deposited in the existing "Reforestation Fund"
to support local project financing (see Annex 8).
16.         Local Enmlgyet.  To provide the local population with a greater
stake in the management ot the Reserve, the project would increase the use of
local workers. Workers would be used in the thinning, protection of natural
regeneration, tree nur-ary, reforestation, road maintenance, survey and Forest
Ranger programs.

V. Prolect Oreanization and Manaaement
19.         Oraanization and Personnel.  All proposed activities would be
implemented by a Pine Forest Reserve Management Unit located at the SRP office
in the "Foret des Pins" village. Management Unit staff would include the
Forest Management Section Read, two Foresters (each responsible for one Forest
Reserve Unit), one Agronomist, and one Forest Ranger Supervisor. Personnel
would include Forestry Technicians, Forest Rangers, Fire Brigades, and
occasional, task specific unskilled labor. A brief description of the major
roles follow:
(a)   Section Head.  The Section Head would be responsible for the
security and management of the entire Pine Forest Leserve and
would report directly to the Chief of the SRF. He would also be
responsible for the preparation of all periodic internal
evaluations and plans. The internationally recruited Senior
Forestry Advisor would assist the Head in the preparation of the
Forest Management Plan. The Head would be responsible for
collaboration with all organizations associated with the
management of the Pine Forest including; SPE, DGI, and the NGO
implementing the local agroforestry services contract. The
incumbent should have an advanced degree in Forest Management;
(b)   Foresters (2).  One Forester would be responsible for each of the
two Forest Units (located in "Mare Rouge", and "Foret des Pins").
Each would report directly to the Unit Head and would supervise
teams of technicians responsible for: reforestation, plantation
management, nursery production, Reserve perimeter survey and
delimitation; forest inventory, and fire control. Each wou
also assist the Chief Forest Ranger in the supervision of Rangers
in their respective Unit. The incumal%tts should have advanced
degrees in Forest Management; and

(c)   Agronomist (1).  The Agronomist would be responsible for on-the-
ground liaison with the contracting NGO, and for the team of
technicians responsiUle for the survey of agricultural lands and
the regulation of land leases.
20.         Administration. Evaluation and Planning.  The Forest Management
Unit would be initially administratively supported from the central SRF
office. All payrolls, fiscalization of the land lease receipts and service
contracts would be administered by the project administrator. The local
capability for contract administration would be developed during the life of
the project. An annual internal program evaluation and planning session would
be held in the Management Unit office at the Foret des Pins village. This two
day event would be attended by the senior Forest Management Unit staff who
would collectively evaluate the past year's accomplishments, weaknesses, and
prepare program targets and plans for the oncoming year. A comprehensive
output monitoring program would also be initiated which would permit the
periodic assessment of performance for all levels of personnel. An annual
technical retreat would also be held where the senior Pine Forest Management
staff would be presented with and debate new technical topics.

Terms of Reference for the Senior Forestry Advisor
1.          Duties:  The incumbent would undertake a three-year assignment,
extendable to five years, in support of the Forestry Resoarce Service (SRF) to
give such advice, information and guidance as may be necessary for the
successful implementation of the Forestry and Environmental Protection Project
(FEPP). He will have an office at the SRF headquarters and will deal directly
with the Chief of the SRP on all aspects of the FEPP related to the SRE. He
will be part of a technical assistance team of five experts. The incumbent
will:
(a)   provide technical input to the Chief of the Forestry Resource
Service for whom he be the counterpart (SRF) and to the separate
Section Heads (I-Forestry Research, 2-Agroforestry Support, 3-Pine
Forest Management, and 4-Forest Statistics). As the number and
complexity of activities are greater in the Pine Forest Section
than the others, the advisor would devote approximately 75% cf his
time on that Section;
(b)   report in personnel administration matters to the leader of the
international assistance team, of which he would be a member;
(c) recommend the use of short-term consultants by the SRF,
receiving/commenting on the terms of reference drafted by the
sep&:ate Seation Heads and the nominated candidates, and ensuring
that specified outputs are produced;
(d)   ensure SRF collaboration with the Environmental Protection Service
(SPE) on the design and implementation of the national land use
assessment and forest inventory, and on the establishment of new
biosphere and forestry reserves;
(e)   assist the Director of the Technical School for Agroforestry
(ENAF) and the affiliated training consultants in designing the
forestry and agrofor2stry curricula, and in implementing a
pragmatic techniciau training program;

(f)   assist the SRF Chief in liaison with other national and
international organizations which could positively impact the
status of forestry development in Haiti or the SRF. This task
would include preparing cooperative agreements on forestry
research and development activities and seeking international
funds for the support of SRI programs;
(g)   contribute to the preparation of semi-annual progress reports for
the SRI and to the preparation of annual vork program and budgets;
and
(h)   assist the SRI in becoming a successful and sustainable

as detailed in the PEPP Appraisal Report, Annex 3;
(b)   quarterly personal progress reports and activity plans and submit
them to the SRI Chief and the FEPP Technical Coordinator; and
(c)   a comprehensive third-phase forestry project, developed with the
full participation of the senior SRI staff.
3.          Oualifications:  The candidate must be able to comprehend written
and spoken French and speak either French or Haitian Creole fluently. She/he
must have a graduate degree in forestry and have had at least 10 years'
professional experience, at least five of which in developing countries. A
candidate with previous, long-term Haitian experience would be preferred.
She/he must have special expertise 'n forest management. Knovledge and
experience in biomass inventory, biosphere reserves, agroforestry, forest
policy and law and rural development would be desirable and an added
advantage. The candidate must have excellene personal relations and
communication skills and be able to work effectively with people of various
backgrounds and at all levels of administration. She/he must also have strong
participatory management skills and be able to lead group evaluations and
program planning sessions.
