HAITI
FORESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
FORESTRY AND AGROFORESTRY RESEARCH
I. Present Situation
l.          Important forestry and agroforestry research has been implemented
by various organizations in Haiti, most notably by the grantees of USAID, FAO
and the SRF. Research is not coordinated on a national scale and the
protocols and results not commonly shared as no institution formally collects
and manages research information.
2.          The rFN initiated research towards the identification of
appropriate exotic species for fuel production on three semi-arid sites (Jean
Rabel, Cabaret, Miragoane). SRF activity on these and other plantation sites
(Colora and Colladere) has been beset by social problems (including regular
sabotage of trials) with the local communities. These problems are due to a
large extent to SRF's ignorance of local concerns and previous utilization of
the appropriated research lands. On-farm, agroforestry demonstrations were
conducted on three sites of varying ecologic conditions.
3.          Though commonly accessed semi-arid forests have historically been
the major source of charcoal products and areas of forest degradation, little
technical or social research activity has been executed in these domains.
Forestry development planners thus understand neither the techniques or social
arrangements required to enhance the productivity of these areas. Large,
under-utilized savannas with apparent substantial forestry potential exist in
central and northern Haiti. No formal research activity has been conducted in
these areas.
II. Proposed Oblectives and Strateav
Objectives
4.          The objective of the forestry and agroforestry research component
is to increase the overall understanding of methods and technologies which
enable the:
(a)   protection and propagation of selected indigenous forest species;
(b)   increased and sustained productivity of Pinus occidentalis within
the Pine Forest Reserve;
(c)   increased and sustained prodtctivity of high altitude agricultural
lands within and adjacent to the La Visite and Pic Nacaya National
?arkG, and the Pine Forest Reserve; and



-84-
ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 8
(d)   increased and sustained productivity of public lands with forestry
vocation (generally savannas and semi-arid forests dominated by
Prosoois and Acacia ape.).
Strategy
5.          The Research Section of the SRF will directly implement forestry
research in semi-arid, savanna and pine forest areas. The Section will
collaborate on the adaptive, on-farm agroforestry research implemented by the
NGOs receiving agroforestry contracts (see Annex 5). The Section would also
collaborate to the extent possible on research directed by other NGOs and
interested international environmental research organizations, and collect
copies of protocols and results of all pertinent research activity being
conducted within the country. The Research Section will complement the
conventional biophysical research efforts with social science research. This
applied research will inform forestry planners as to the social context of the
investigated forestry technologies and the institutional mechanisms required
for technology adoption.
III. Pro1ect Components
Forestry Research
6.          Pine Forest Research.  The following research activities would be
conducted in the Pine Forest Reserve by the Forest Management staff (see
Annex 3) under the supervision of the Forestry Research Section:
(a) a tree improvement program would be initiated with Pinus
occidentalis in collaboration with the SECID Seed and Germplasm
Project, including provenance trials;
(b)   entomology research would be initiated towards identification of
appzopriate integrated pest management methods to control the
defoliating Saturn moth;
(c)   comparative species trials would be initiated with Pinus sp. from
similar ecologic sites (as initiated by the SECID project near
Kenscoff in 1989);
(d) P. occidentalis seed conservation, treatment and scarification
trials would be conducted;
(e)   thinning trials would be initiated on naturally regenerated P.
occidentalis stands to determine optimal thinning regimes; and
(f) permanent plots would be established to monitor stand growth and
mortality.



-85-
Page 3 of 8
Agroforestrv Research
7.          Aaroforestry Research in the Pine Forest.  The objectives of this
research would be to investigate and develop sustainable land use technologies
appropriate for areas of high elevation (800-1,500 m). Special emphasis
would be placed on identifying interventions for steep slopes (30-60Z). The
SRF Research Unit would collaborate with the NGO implementing the agroforestry
subproject in the Pine Forest Reserve to conduct the research. The precise
role and responsibility of each agency would be determined during the
MARNDR/NGO contract negotiation process.
8.          Agroforestry research in the Pine Forest would be initiated with a
formal assessment of local agricultural technology, knowledge and attitudes.
The research agenda would be de'eloped with local farmers based upon their
identified agricultural needs. It should include investigation of shade
tolerant crops, multiple-use perennials and fruit tree species and would be
generally transferable to other high elevation areas in Haiti. The Unit would
promote and complement local technological innovation and independent farmer
experimentation.
Land Management for Forestry Production Research
9.          Fuelwood Production Research.  Ongoing fuelwood production
research on the existing Cabaret, Jean Rabel, Miragoane, Colora and Colladere
stations would be continued. In addition to this technical research, social
research would be initiated where local farmers would be integrated into the
management of the research lands (as has been initiated on the Colora
station). The purpose of this social research would be to learn of the
strengths and weaknesses of various management options for public lands.
Results from the technical research would be extended to interested adjacent
farmers with adaptive, on-farm forestry trials. This effort would be
conducted as "adaptive research", the purpose being to 'Learn of the
opportunities and constraints of the technologies generated, and of the
extension of those technologies.
10          Savanna Reforestation Pilot Proaram.  Approximately 30,000 ha of
under-utilized public savanna lands exist in the Central Plateau. Roughly
another 10,000 ha are located in northeastern Haiti. These lands are
characterized by low population density, negligible agronomic activity,
seasonal and limited livestock grazing, annual fires, and apparent forestry
potential. The purpose of the reforestation would be to produce wood products
at amounts exceeding local demand. The excess production could thus be
exp%.rted to urban markets. It is expected that charcoal would be made of the
waste materials from the production of primary products. The direct
conversion of trees to charcoal might be desirable if reforesetation costs are
comparatively low, propagation techniques are simple and easily adopted, and
charcoal prices rise significantly in the near-term.
11.         Objectives:  The objectives of the Savanna Pilot Research Program
are tot



-86-
ANNEX 4
Page 4 of 8
(a)   initiate SRP presence and technical reforestation research in the
Savanna Diane and Manigat de Clay savanna areas; and
(b)   learn of the institutional mechanisms required to achieve
reforestation in the savanna areas beyond the immediate research
site (extension methods, land tenure arrangements, incentives).
12.         Methodt    The Research Section would select approximately 200 ha
of land in the Biliguy area of Savanna Diane and another 200 ha on the Savanna
Manigat de Clay. The Section would initially conduct formal aGsessments of
the patterns, rights and duties of local access and utilization of the
proximate savanna lands on State lands. A site would then be selected,
surveyed, and delimited. The project would conduct the research activities in
a manner that integrates rather than repudiates previous local access and use.
The project would utilize local labor sources for the station research. The
SRI Research Unit would directly implement the technical research activities
both on the identified station and on adjacent farms. Technical assistance
and tree seedlings would be provided to interested, adjacent farmers.
13.         Research:
(a)   technical:  the Research Section would conduct species and
provenance trials at various densities and configurations, and
with various site preparation treatments. Trial tree species
would be chosen upon their potential to produce high valued wood
products (posta, poles, lumber). Trials would thus include both
indigenous and exotic species such as Swietenia mahoaani.
Swietenia mahogani x Swietenia macroDhvlla, Cuazama ulmifolia,
Simarouba SpB., Cassia siamea, Azadirachta indica, Acacia
auriculiformis, Eucalvytus aps., Casuarina sps., Leucaena
leucocephala, Leucasena leucoceDhala x Leucaena diversifolia, and;
(b)   social:  the Section would experiment with several different
tenurial arrangements with local farmers within the research site.
These arrangements would include: provision of long-term leases to
defined blocks of land, and the collective management of land
units. These provisions would be made on the condition of
reforestation efforts by the lessee. Methods of managing the
leases and use of incentives would be investigated thoroughly.
14.         Semi-Arid Forest Management Research.  Natural Prosonis 1uliflora
stands dominate approximately 300,000 ha of degraded, semi-arid, public and
private forest land in Haiti. Major stands are located in the northwest,
south-central, and southwest areas of the country. Presence of authority is
often very limited and thus the lands are often commonly accessed and
exploited.
15.         Though they have historically accounted for a majority of the wood
used for charcoal production, foresters have largely ignored P. iuliflora and
its common companion species, Acacia tortuosa. Preliminary evidence indicates



-87-
ANNEX 4
Page 5 of 8
that on average sites, production from the xerophytic stands average
1.5 m/ha/year without management and 5 m3/ha/year with management.
Successful management of P. 1ulif1ota in sylvopastoral systems for post
products, forage  nd livestock shelter is found in proximity to farmer homes
in several areas of Haiti.
16.         The semi-arid forests are one of Haiti's last renewable resources
with substantial productive potential. As stems readily coppice and extensive
native stands exist, no planting or site preparation costs are required.
Semi-arid forest management presents then a low cost option for increasing and
sustaining the production of wood products in Haiti.
17.         Objectives:  The objectives of the Semi-Arid Forest Management
Pilot Program are to:
(a)   conduct forest management experiments toward increasing the
production of wood biomass and high valued economic products from
semi-arid forests;
(b)   determine the social institution mechanisms which would enable the
sustained productivity of the commonly accessed semi-arid stands
in both the private and public domains; and
(c)   conduct sylvopastoral experiments toward the determination of
optimum management systems and stocking levels which increase both
wood and livestock production from the dry land forests.
18.         Method:   In the first year of the project, ihe SRF Research Unit
would icentify and investigate candidate research sites. If the
identification of an adequate site where both social and technical research
could be conducted proves problematic, two sites could be selected, one for
the technical research (which would require regular monitoring) and another
site for the social research. Both sites chosen should meet the following
criteria:
(a)   total approximately 200 ha and contain natural semi-arid forest
cover which is representative of semi-arid forest formations that
remain on public lands in Haiti (e.g., stand age, 3-5 years; 4-5
coppice stems (of 5-10 cm) per stump; 10-16 stumps per 100 m2);
and
(b)   be in the state domain (public or private) and be free of official
leaseholders.
The site chosen for the technical research should be regularly accessible by
4-wheel drive vehicle. The Cul de Sac Plain and Conaives areas should be
investigated for this site. The island of La Gonave, Gonaives, and the
Northwestern area of Haiti should be investigated for placement of the site.
19.         The Unit would initially conduct formal socio-economic assessments
of the site and adjacent areas to understand the dynamics of current access



-88-
AfNNEX 4
Page 6 of 8
and land use. Both technical and social research agendas and protocols would
correspond directly to the patterns of land use, knowledge and technology
evidenced by the assessment. In addition to direct research, in time the
project would also work directly with adjacent farmers, providing technical
assistance. SRP would directly implement research and also collaborate with
other institutions working on dry land forest management such as FONDEV, and
Texas A & I University.
20.         Research:
(a)   technical:   biomase volume, coppice management and other
pertinent research would be conducted. The Unit would also
initiate sylvopastoral trials, using different stocking,
management and pasture improvement schemes. Research would
include the investigation of traditional semi-arid forest
management for livestock shelter, post products and charcoal.
Examples of these "home-garden" management systems are found in
the Cul de Sac Plain near Thomazeau. The project would support
the P. iuliflora genetic improvement work implemented by Texas
A & I University, establishing on-station trials and reproduction
centers; and
(b)   social:  the project would research existing semi-arid forest use
patterns and initiate various land management schemes towards the
improvement of forest productivity. These trials would include
the provision of long-term leases, fencing and the collective
management of lands by organizat4.ons of current users. Incentives
such as land leases could be conditional upon participating
farmers utilization of authorized land management practices. The
SRF would also continue the ongoing biomass study with
P. iuliflora and Acacia spa. in Jean Rabel and Cabaret.
Research Document Collection and Management
21.         The SRP Research Unit would collaborate with all other
institutions conducting forestry and agroforestry research in Haiti. This
collaboration would entail active cooperation on the development of protocols,
analysis of findings and the preparation of research agendas. The Unit woAld
collect and manage all pertinent research information.

Savanna Diane Pilot Research Site Location
1.          The proposed site for the pilot research station is located
approximately 14 km southeast of St. Michel de L'Attallaye in the vicinity of
Habitation Casseus. The exact location of this site will be contingent upon
detailed on-site studies which would determine if local soils and topography
are representative of state lands in Savanna Diane. Site selection will also
depend upon existing land tenure and utilization situation as identified in
the initial socio-economic assessment. The Casseus area is promising because
of its relative accessibility and its diverse soil types. In this arer it is
possible to delineate a 200 ha block of land that encompasses 5-6 soil
groups/subgroups (as described by the Carte de Sols-Savanna Diane/SCET
International/PNUD/OPE/Janvier 1990) and three major bioclimatic zones (as
described by the Carte Bioclimatic in the FAO project report, Projet Forets et
Protection de L'Environnement, Annexe 4, Carte 3) which are representative of
much of the land available for forestry plantations.
Savanna Manigat de Clay Pilot Research Site Location
2.          The proposed site for the research station is located
approximately 14 km south of Fort Liberte near the village of Acul Samedi.
The exact location of this site will be contingent upon detailed on-site
studies which would determine if site soils and topography are representative
of the Manigat de Clay savanna lands within the public domain. Site selection
will also depend upon the existing land tenure and utilization patterns as
identified in the initial socio-economic assessment.

FO TRSTY AND ENVIRONMNTAL PROTETIO  PRUOJECT
Asroforestrv REtencIn
I.  Present Sitation IL and Rear Prems
1.          Forest ecosystem In Haiti are under regular oncroachment pressure
from adjacont farmers. This situation is especially precarious in the cases
of the Pic Macaya and La Visits National Park. and the Pine Forest Reserve
where lands within the reserve boundaries can be agriculturally productive.
Principal causes for this encroachment are diminishing agricultural yields and
population growth in adjacent areas. The Oovernmont of Haiti has maintained a
policy and small police force which ostensibly exclude encroachers from the
legally protected areas. This approach, which treats adjacent populations as
adversaries rather than partners has had limited success. Adjacent
populations will continue to threaten Important ecosystems until their
livelihood is enhanced and they gain a direct benefit in the protection of the
ecosystems.
II. Progosed Objectives and Strategy
Obi ectives
2.          The objectives of the agroforaw try production component are to:
(a)   stabilize and increase agricultural production on adjacent
holdings so as to limit agricultural expansion into the reserve
and preserve areas and improve the quality of life of rural
peoples who live in proximity to such protective areas;
(b)   increase the supply of wood products to both local and urban
marketst and
(c)   institutionalize KARKDR/NGO cooperation on forestry projects and
simultaneously increase HARNDR capabilities to plan, coordinate
and monitor natural resource development activities in Haiti.
Strateev
3.          The provision of agroforestry production services to peasants
farming in and near protected reserve and preserve areas would be contracted
to qualified NGOs.  Ministry of Agriculture representatives would monitor and             L
evaluate sub-project activities as per the negotiated contract agreement. As
contractors, the NGOs would operate independently of MAUDR, submitting annual
reports and program plans for authorization by FEPP officials. Upon contract
approval, the contractor would receive a mobilization advance to permit the



-104-
Page 2 of 14
acquisition of equipment and the location of personnel. Regular withdraw
requests would be submitted to the PePP adminiotrator for expense
reimbursement.
LII1. Prolect Comnent
4.          Four sub-projects would be contracted to qualified
non-governmental organizations for Implementation during the PEPP. NGOs eousld
bid on one or more sub-project, and combine sub-projects into a single project
as they deem appropriate. In this case, O Ns would describe this combination
in their project proposalo The sub-projects are as follow.s
(a)   agroforestry extension, including the promotion of a pilot program
for cistern construction, in the Pine Forest Reserve and adjacent
buffer zonel
(b)   agroforestry extension In the Pic Macaya National Park and
the Northern adjacent buffer zone;                                           r
(c)   agroforestry extension in the La Visite National P&rk and
adjacent buffer zones and
(d)   agroforeetry extension In the Belle Anse region of Southeast
Haiti.
5.          The Pine Forest Reserve and La Visite ?ark projects are planned as
five-year projects, beginning in Year 1 of tCe PEPP. The Pic Macaya Park and
the Belle Anse Region agroforestry projects are planned as four-year projects,
beginning in Year 2 of the PBPP. The Euvironmental Protection Service (SPE),
responsible for all National Park activities, would supervise the sub-projects
in and around both National Parks. The Forestry Resource Service (SRP) would
supervise the Pine Forest and Belle Anse sub-projects. The agroforestry
extension projects in and around the Pine Forest Reserve and the National
Parks would b: intensive and integrative In approach, providing multiple
services to resatively small populations. The Belle Anse Region extension
project would use a more extensive approach, providing limited so.vices (tree
seedlings, hedgerow technology and technical assistance) to a greater
population in the higher elevations. Terms of reference for each st..-project
are in the Appendices 1 to 4 to this Annex.
Deagriotion of Pro1ect Areas and Farmin Svstems
6.          Pine Forest and La Visite project areas share many of the same
ecological and climatic conditions. Consequently, their agricultural
production systems are, quite similar. Taking advantage of the cool moist
conditions as well as access to markets in Port-au-Prince peasant farmers are
progressively shifting from cultivation of traditional crops (corn, beans,
sweet potatoes) to higher valued market vegetables (potatoes, onions, cabbage,
carrots, loeks). While the cultivation of these new crops generate greater



-105-
Page 3 of 14
revenues for the farmer, they also require greater expenditures, in both labor
and capital. Increasingly farmers are utilizing expensive external inputs,
such as chaemicl and organic fertilizer, seed, and pestlides, and are
contracting additional labor to establish and maintain these crops. Despite
the increased prevalence of market vegetable cropping, most farmers are forced
by economic constraints end risk management to keop a good portion of their
land in traditional crops, grain and legume crops.
7.          Farmers market most of their vegetable production and some of
their other crops in Port-au-Prince or other less distant market centers.
While the La Visit. project area is closer to the capital, lack of a direct
road access requires that crops be transported on foot or by animal to
Kenskoff or south to Harigot.
8.          As is the case throughout Haiti, farmers in the project areas
typically own or utilize several parcels of land and manage crop production
across all the plots. The primary plot, which contains the farmers house,
often is utilized as a household garden producing small amounts of coffee,
fruit, vegetables, plantains, yams, etc. Fields close to the house are often
planted to higher valued and management intensive crops such as potatoes and
onions. Fields far from the compound are typically planted to corn and beans
and are periodically put under fallow. Land tenure in the Pine Forest and La
Visite areas varies considerably. A majority of land surrounding the Pine
Forest Reserve is private land ("to don" or "te eritaj"), while except for a
small number of private holdings in the Seguin area, the vast majority of land
surrounding the La Visits Park is state land. This land is currently leased
to small Uolders by the Direction G6n6rale des Imp8ts.
9.          Declining farm productivity is a critical problem faced by farmers
in all project areas. Inappropriate farming practices lead to soil
exhaustion, bigh levels of pest damage, and high rates of soil erosion.
According to A0, 60S of agricultural land in the Pine Forest is over 30S
slope (32Z of the land is over 452 slope).1' Lack of soil conservation
technologies result in heavy soil loss and declining crop yields, thereby
increasing expansion of the agricultural frontier and perpetuating low
standards of living.
10.         Livestock is an Important element of the peasant farming system in
all project areas. In the Pine Forest over 80S of farm households practice
animal husbandry, with cattle, sheep, goats and pigs comprising the most.11
Grazing of livestock generally is done on land under fallow or on state land.
)1 lAO Rapport de Pr6paration, Projet ForOta et Protection de
L'Environnoment, Annex 5, Appendice 1, Tableau 3. 21/90/CP-HlAI 19.
14 f6vrier 1990.
2/ Ibid. Annee 5, Appendice 2, Tableau 7.



-106-
Page 4 of 14
Lack of forage is common, especially in the dry season, and farmers must
expend considerable time and energy searching for adequate sites to tie their
animals. Another constraint to improving livestock production are reliable
supplies of water for the animals.
11.         Farmers in the project areas exert considerable pressure on
adjacent Reserve and Preserve lands. The expansion of farm plots contributes
significantly to loss of forest cover. Inappropriate use of fire on-farm
often leads to uncontrolled grass and forest fires, damaging adult trees and
eliminating natural regeneration. Pine post and poles which are used for
fences and buildings are in short supply but high demand in both the Pine
Forest and La Visite areas. There are restrictions on the harvest of live
but not dead trees. This policy provides incentive for the setting of
wildfires which kill the imiature trees of post size. The illegal cutting of
pine trees for timber is most frequently financed by wealthier Individuals
living outside of the forested areas. The harvesting of bwa gra or pine
fatwood severely damages trees and contributes to windthrow. This practice is
likewise dominated by outsiders but poorer local fumilies may participate
during the dry season. High livestock stocking levels in several areas result
in overgrazing and site degradation through erosion. Animals also inhibit
natural regeneration of pines and other species on state and private land.
12.         Farm production in Pic Macava National Park project area is based
predominantly on beans, sweet potatoes, corn and yams. Other crops include
cassava, malanga, plantains, coffee and some vegetables. Crop yields tend to
be low, especially for corn which is often severely damaged by a fungal
infection (Phvllachora Maydis), Poor access to markets is a major constraint
faced by farmers in the park and buffer zone area. Most produce is carried to
Lee Cayes or other markets by pack animal or porter, reducing farmgate prices
and limiting crops that can be marketed.
13.         The Belle Anse area encompasses a wide variety of climatic and
soil types, and consequently, several cropping systems are found. At the low
elevations (below 200 i), settlements are concentrated along the coast (e.g.,
the town of Belle Anse), the climate is semi-arid and inhabitants depend to a
large extent on fishing (and more recently charcoal production) for their
livelihood but also plant sorghum and pigeon pea on land cleared of the thorny
vegetation. Irregular rainfall, however, reduces crop yields and often causes
total crop failure. Above 200 m rainfall increases and agriculture becomes
more stable. With increasing elevation sorghum cultivation gives way to corn,
beans and root crops. Coffee cultivation likewise increases with elevation,
reaching a maximum at 800-900 m in the area of Tiotte. In this coffee belt
yams and beans are also planted in small clearings. Steeper hillsides are
often planted to annual crops such as corn and sorghum. Increasingly farmers
are converting coffee lands to annual crop production because of declining
coffee prices and yields, and rising charcoal prices. Upon clearing the
coffee and shade trees, yams and beans are typically planted to take advantage



-107-
Page 5 of 14
of the high organic content and fertility of the soils. Erosion and heavy
cropping reduce yields quickly and result in a shift to grains and legumes.
Above 1,000 m production of market vegetables is more common where there is
access to markets (close to roads).
14.         The Belle Ans  agroforestry project would concentrate it.
intervention in the higher elevationa in areas adjacent to those which are
targeted by the La Visits and Pine Forest projects. These areas can be
accessed by the roads leading to the Baie %;'Orange, B1l Airs, and Corail
eommunities from the town of Belle Anse. The Mapou and Bodarie communities
(along the road from Thiotte to Belle Anse) are other access points to the
upper elevation areas.
Potential for Development
15.         In order to abhieve the objectives of alleviating pressure on
ilaiti's national parka and other critical ecosystem areas, it is crucial that
programs be developed to help farmers in targeted areas to rationalize the
natural resource use through improved agricultural development. Local farmers
must be provided with technologies and training which will both improve their
steadily deteriorating economic situation on-farm and reduce their negative
impact on protected resources off-farm. If it is to be effective, this
technological package must progressively lead to:
(a)   a reversal of soil productivity declines through soil conservation
measures and improved farming systems;
(b)   the adoption of agroforestry systems that increase on-farm
production of forest products and agricultural crops;
(c)   increased farm productivity through improved crop husbandry,
harvesting and storage techniques, improved crop varieties and
better marketing;
(d)   improved animal husbandry through increased forage production,
better disease control, and livestock management; and
(e)   the construction of reliable water supplies for human and animal
consumption and the irrigation of nurseries and vegetable gardens.
16.         Soil Conservation and Management.  Farmers in the targeted project
areas could benefit greatly from the adoption of biological soil conservation
measures, such as contour hedgerows (with grass or woody species), check dams



-108-
Page 6 of 14
in ravines (using live posts or rocks), and the use of cover crops. These
technologies have been successfully promoted by NGOs throughout Raiti.1'
Given the proper training and germplasm support farmers have adopted these
techniques whi:h have helped control soil losses, increase soil structure and
fertility, and provide animal fodder. These technologies, which are low cost
and relatively simple to manage, Improve the sustainability of project
initiated benefits, and the potential adoption and diffusion of techniques
beyond the iediate project borders.
17.         Agroforestr=.  Trees should play a contral role in developing
sustainable and productive farming systems within project areas. Border
planting of fast growing hardwood trees will provide farmers with new cash
crops which complement, rather than displace, ongoing agricultural activities.
Trees can provide the farmer with a flexible income source, hervested or left
to grow, if need be. In addition to increasing income, on-farm wood
production can reduce pressure on nearby natural forests for fuel and building
materials. Farmers will be provided training on appropriate planting patterns
and management techniques that will minimize tree competition with
agricultural crops. Border tree plantings will also provide protection from
strong winds that are common in the high elevation project areas. Improved
varieties of fruit trees will provide farmers with fruit for household
consumption and for off-farm marketing.
18.         Improved Crop Productivitv.  Low yields of traditional crops will
be addressed through the provision of training on improved cultivation
techniques (contour furrowing, "pike te" or deep soil cultivation, organic
matter and mulch additions) and through the introduction of improved varieties
of traditional crops as well as trial introduction of new crops (vegetables,
fruit, spices). Farmers will be trained in improved storage techniques that
can reduce post-harvest losses which currently are significant. Improved
storage capacity will enable farmers to hold some farm products off the market
until periods of lower supply, and hence, higher prices.
19.         Improved Anlmal Husbandrv.  The adoption of the soil conservation
(hedgerows) and agrow.restry interventions will significantly ir.crease the
supply of forage and thereby increase the carrying capacity of the farmers'
land holdings. As part of the training package, farmers will be encouraged to
begin stall feeding efforts for household livestock. This practice can
provide benefits in terms of higher animal productivity, production of manure
for application to vegetable crops, and a general lessening of grazing
pressure on state lands. To improve the general efficiency of animal

materials, and the organization of operation and maintenance. The cisterns
would be constructed by contractors hired by the DRN.
Proaram Develoyment Strategy
21.         The agroforesty extension programs will be based on the following
design considerations:
(a)   agroforestry extension programs would utilize and promote local
personnel to as great extent as feasible to ensure that extension,
technical and management skills remain in the communities beyond
the project term;
(b)   programs would identify and work through existing farmer and
community groups or associations. Efforts should be made to
strengthen these groups, involving them In problem solving,
technology development and program planning, implementation and
evaluation processes;
(c)   special attention should be given to the development of
appropriate and sustainable incentive packages for farmers (e.g.,
encourage investment in hedgerows). Programs will focus on
creating a local demand for technologies rather than relying on
monetary or commodity incentives for adoption. While it will be
necessary at the outset to provide farmers with the necessary
germplasm to - tablish border plantings and hedgerows, attempts
must be made    .y in the project to encourage sustainable on-farm
production of germplasm inputs, l.e., on-farm tree seedling
production, improved fruit trees through on-site grafting, and
production and maintenance of improved crop germplasm; and
(d)   reforestation and revegetat1 ' efforts within the National Parks
would be consistent with tht goals of natural ecosystem protection
and thus focus on the utilization '0 endemic species (e.g.,
Ji&Mgerus eckmanii). Threatened bu. economically valuable species
would also be promoted in the buffer areas of both the parks and
the Pine Forest Reserve.



-110-
Page 8 of 14
22.         Tradiltonal PaLsant Groups. Agricultural task-based peasant
groupingag, of various functions and titleas, are a common traditional and
current feature of rural Raiti. These groups are generally "informal," yet
rules and sanctions concerning participation are frequently established.
Groups associated with exchange labor for land clearing, planting, weeding and
harvesting tasks are of great prevalence and renown in Haiti. Other groups
exist to execute single or periodic collective tasks such as manioc
processing, house ralsing, irrigation canal building, etc. Market women also
sometimes transport goods together, or provide monetary support to one
another. Groups function for reasons of both member utility and solidarity,
as periodic labor scarcity, decreasing disposable income, and social
approbation are strong motivations to work collectively rather than
individually.
23.         Grouo Extension Exberience in Previous Natural Resource
Conservation Projects. Since the early 1980a rural development projects1'
have mobilized peasants for development activities via peasant organization
strategies. These projects have generally Initiated activities by identifying
the frequency and nature of traditional peasant groupings and then sought to
"formalize" them with training In basic organizational skills. These groups
also become active participants In the agriculture, soil conservation, or
forestry activities. Such projects also encourage the formation of new groups
where conditions are propitious. The reported advantages of a group-based
extension strategy are several and include the following: (i) the group is an
active, problem solving forum which can simultaneously educate and motivate
membersl (ii) group dynamics can facilitate farmer's authentic participation
in project activities and a sense of "ownership' in those activities;
(III) groups are not only efficient vehicles for extension themes, but can
positively affect technique adoption rates as collective adoption diminishes
the perceived risk to the individual member; (iv) extension through groups
increaoses cooperation and coordination skills which subsequently increase the
probability that labor intensive measures such as ravine check-dams will be
adopted; and complex social dilemmas such as common property and trans-
boundary soil erosion problem can be solved; and (v) groups are "sustainable"
A/ The Catholic Relief Service, Gros Morne Project and the "Moveman
Paysan Papay" (MPP) Project pioneered group-based extension and
group formation methods. More recently other NGOs such as the
Mennonite Central Comittes (MCC), CARE, Helvetas, and Save the
Children Federation (SCF) have used peasant groups both as vehicles
for the extension of technical innovations and as the foundations
for community development activities. Some projects have more
recently noticed the spontaneous formation of social innovations,
such as the "brigad" in the MPP area which are self-selected
and self-named farmer groups who share labor strictly for the
establishment of soil conservation and agroforestry measures.



-111-
NEX 5
Page 9 of 14
organizations which can facilitate the continued adoption and diffusion of
project promoted behaviors and the development of innovations beyond project
termination.
24.         The Role of Farmer Grouns in the Agroforestrv Extension Programs.
The Pic Macaya Park, La Visite Park and Pine Forest Reserve projects would use
a group-oriented extension strategy, using groups as the principal vehicles
for extension messages and the basic units for other promoted development
activities. Though groups should be targeted, projects would also provide
extension services to individuals and would not base access to project benefit
upon group membership. Trees would be planted and soil conservation
structures would be installed by participating individuals or groups on
privately held lands or agricultural lands leased from the State. Individual
peasants would be guaranteed all harvest, usufruct and propagation rights to
the trees, planted during the life of their lease. If a lease was canceled,
the farmer would lose rights to the trees planted. The projects would not
promote collective tree ownership or management except if in future the SRE
chooses to allocate Pine Forest Reserve tracts to local management for local
benefit. The role of the groups would be to inform the project as to local
prerogatives, participate in the development and extension of the promoted
technologies and activities, seek the resolution of local problems, and assist
in project evaluations. In the Pine Forest area, particular focus would be
given to a pilot program for improvement of water supplies by farmer groups.
The Belle Anse Agroforestry project would not directly target farmer groups or
explicitly encourage the formation of new groups. This project would follow
the USAID Agroforestry Outreach Project model, extending limited, and clearly
defined agroforestry services to a large area.   Its explicit goal is the
planting of trees and the installment of agroforestry measures on private
lands by individual farmers. The project would implicitly support local
organizations to the extent feasible by actively encouraging the establishment
of local tree nurseries and farmer cooperation on the installment of labor
intensive techniques.
25.         Aaroforestry Extension and Peasant Oraanization Strategv.  The
NGOs implementing the La Visite Park, Pic Macaya Park and the Pine Forest
Reserve Programs would generally follow the integrated resource conservation
project models provided by Helvetas/MPP, CARE/Leger, MCC, and SCF/Iaissade.
The NGOs would be granted flexibility in their establishment of extension and
organization strategies as they will undoubtedly had previous experience in
extension and peasant organization, and as strategies should correspond both
to local conditions and to NGO characteristics. The following agroforestry
extension and peasant organization method is presented as a general guide and
a suggested point of departure for more detailed and site specific schemes.
NGO would detail their specific methods in their separate project proposals.
(a)   Each program would initially conduct a rapid rural assessment to
identify salient local physical and social parameters including
the type and patterns of peasant groupings.



-112-
Page 10 of 14
(b)   Programs would identify local candidates for exten.ion positions
("animate") either by local popular election or by direct project
selection. These individuals would be trained in peasant
organization and/or agroforestry techniques.
(c)   Extension agents would return to their respective communities to
conduct participatory local appraisals to identify and diagnose
local production, marketing and consumption patterns, and
constraints to development. Appraisals would also include the
discussion of preliminary local solutions and strategies for the
resolution of local problems.
(d)   Agents would identify existing local social groups (including
labor exchange groups, pre-established farmer groups, and
community associations) and provide education on basic
organizational themes, and methods to initiate collective economic
activities. Technical assistance to the groups would begin only
after groups have thoroughly examined local needs and requested
project assistance. Extension agents would not actively "create"
new groups, but would assist self-formed groups formalize. Other
new groups would spontaneously form in successive years as they
witness the activities and benefits gained through greater
cooperation and implementation of project techniques.
(e)   Groups or representatives from them would be given training in
agroforestry, soil conservation techniques as well as other
agricultural training deemed appropriate by the project.
(f)   Projects would continue to strengthen the groups (both pre-
existent and newly formed) encouraging their association and
advancement to more complex resource conservation and community
development projects. These participating groups would also be
encouraged to extend the messages beyond project boundaries.

Technical Interventions and Model Assumptions
28.         Soil Conservation.  For slopes up to 20S specific soil
conservation measures are not economical.11 It is assumed that slopes of
less than 301 would not be targeted directly by the hedgerow interventions.
Farmers on these slopes will be trained in basic contour farming techniques,
green manure and other improved agricultural practices which should edequately
control erosion and fertility-related productivity declines.
29.         Targeted hillside farms in the extension areas are assumed to be
located on high and steep slopes (30-60X) that are rapidly being degraded by
erosion. Because of slope and soil conditions crop yields on these farms are

and tree hedgerows.   The hedges will be spaced 10 m apart and would have a
total length of 1,000 m/ha. According to early estimates by BDPA 7/ sheet
erosion on cultivated slopes of 45-601 results in the decrease of crop yields
by 2.51 each year. Ravines caused by erosion also lead to the loss of 0.31 of

32.         Croo Imnrovemsnt.  All extension projects, with the excepti,  of
Belle Anse, would provide farmers with improved maize and bean seed during
their first year of participation. Other crops are not considered because of
the difficulty of propagation and disemoination of the crop (sweet potatoes)

pesticides and Improved seed) for the market vegetables. Farmers provided
with improved maize and bean seed can expect to increase yields on these crops
by 202 in the first year of use.
33.         Tree Cronping.  It is assumed that farmers participating in the
agroforestry extension programs would plant 125 fast-growing trees along the
borders of their fields (2.5 m apart). These trees would have a 60X survival
rate and be harvested at Year 5 for poles and charcoal. Harvests would yield
75 poles (3 m, valued at US$0.40 each) and a total of 96 kg (2.5 sacks) of
charcoal. At lower elevations (Belle Anse area) growth rates will increase to
produce 1.5 kg charcoal per tree at harvest. Trees will resprout and be
harvested again at year 10 and 15.21 It is assumed that competition by
border trees with field crops will be offset by a reduction in wind-related
desiccation and crop damage.
34.         Participating farmers in project areas would receive 10 grafted
fruit trees ranging from citrus and avocado to improved fruits such as peaches
and apples. These trees will begin to bear fruit after three years and be in
full production around seven years.  Their value at maturity is estimated at
$2.50/year per tree.
Analysis of Farm Models
35.         Farm Models.  Prices of farm inputs and outputs and production
costs per ha and per crop are presented in Table 5.1 of this Annex. Detailed
analyses of the six models are in Tables 5.2 to 5.7 of this Annex. The
financial impact of the proposed project interventions on the different farm
models varies considerably. The incremental net incomes depend much on the
11/ Based on the experience of ORE in Pwoje Sove Te (see ORE Annual Report

assumptions on the speed of the further degradation of the soils due to
progressing erosion. The technical package with tree cropping only (Farm
Model 3A has the lowest returns, due to the long gestation period of the
trees, but also has the lowest project cost per farmer. All models are
characterized by the introduction of low cost improvements relying mainly on
the use of increased labor use and improved inputs (seeds, seedlings) that are
initially subsidized by the project, which do not negatively affect the
farmers's cash flow and thus do not require credit. The returns to farmers
under these models are probably understated. All labor Inputs are valued at
market value (US$1.00/man-day) when in practice a significant proportion of
labor inputs can be applied during periods of low labor demand when wages are
lower. The projection that hedgerow interventions would not be installed on
slopes less than 30S is also conservative as is the 10 crop production
increase. Tree cropping calculations assume that trees will be harvested at
Year 5 for pole and charcoal production. It is often the case that farmers
will postpone harvest in order to produce posts, timber or ether higher valued
wood products. Were these benefits, or reduced costs, to be quantified, they
would provide greater insight into why farmers throughout Haiti have
undertaken tree planting and soil conservation efforts.
36.         Adoption Rates.  The estimated adoption rates for the different
farm models by extension area is in Table 5.8 of this Annex. The extension
programs in the Pine Forest and La Visit Park areas would start in Year one of
the project, while the programs for the Pic Macaya Park and the Belle Anse
area would start in Year 2, because lead time is needed to prepare the
institutional capacity in the Ministry to supervise the programs. A total of
about 16,400 farmers out of a total estimated farm number of 45,200 in the
program areas are expected to adopt the farm improvements. This corresponds
to about 36X of the farmers in the extension areas.
37.         Production ImDact and Prolect Benefits.  Because of the
uncertainties regarding the assumption of production declines without the
project on these particular farms, and regarding the estimated adoption rates,
a detailed analysis of the production impact and the incremental financial and
economic benefits is highly speculative.

FORESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
Aaroforestrv Extension In th fine Forest
Reserve and Adlacent Buffer Area
Terms of Regferteo
1.        Purnoses  Enhancement of local welfare and the control of
agricultural expansion. This project would Increase aSriculture and wood
production on state lands leased by peasants within Unit 1 of the Pine Forest
Reserve and on private lands in the adjacent buffer area. The project would
serve to catalyze the local capacity to sustain increases in local
agricultural productivity beyond project life and, simultaneously develop the
capability of local institutions to manage local development activities. It
would also implement a pilot program for water resource development in the
extension areas (construction of cisterns), using construction contractors
hired by the Natural Resources Directorate (DRN), and contributions in labor
and/or local materials by the benefitting farmers.
2.        The extension contractor would work independent of, but under the
supervision of the Ministry of Agriculture's Directorate of Natural Resources
(DRN) and its Forestry Resource Service (SRP). The contractor would also
collaborate at the field level with the SRP Pine Forest Reserve Management
Unit. This SRF Unit, charged with ensuring the security and proper management
of the Forest is also charged with the administration of Reserve lands leased
to peasants. The contractor would complement the SRF technical forest
management by providing agroforestry extension services to peasant farmers
within and adjacent to the Pine Forest Reserve. The extension contractor
would also identify potential water development subprojects, mobilize the
beneficiaries, contribute to planning and construction supervision, and assist
the beneficiaries in the later operation and maintenance of the cisterns.
3.        Duration:  The project would be implemented for five years,
beginning in FY 1991/92 and terminating 1996. The contract would be initially
for three years, extendable to five years, subject to the results of the mid-
term evaluation of the entire Forestry and Environmental Protection Project.
4.        Locations  Unit I of the Pine Forest Reserve is approximately 75 km
southeast of Port-au-Prince and situated along the crest of the Massif de La
Selle. The village of "Foret des Pins" is located near the center of Unit 1.
The buffer zone would include areas within approximately .5 miles of the
Reserve boundary and include the communities of Burassa, Savane Mouton,
Oriani, and Gros Cheval.



-126-
ANNL 5
Appendix I
Page 2 of 5
5.        ProblelM Satement:  The total population vithin the Unit 1 of the
Pine Forest Roserve is approximately 6,500. Another 5,000 are estimated to
live within .5 miles of the Reserve boundary. Approximately 1,500 parcels of
Reserve lands are leased to farmers yet regular encroachment beyond these
parcels into the forest zones destroys the natural forest stands and further
degrades the soil resources. Until recently, great irregularity existed in
lease conditions and officially, each lease vas to be renewed annually upon
rent payment. This situation resulted in an Insecure land tenure situation,
and scarce incentive existed for the lessees to invest in substantial
conservationist treatments and the planting of perennials. Reserve
inhabitants have also until recently received no legal direct benefit from the
Pine Forest or technical and development assistance from the government. A
generally antagonistic relationship exists between the inhabitants and the
Forestry Resources Service.
6.        Since 1989 the Pine Forest Management Unit has conducted a program
to survey leased lands and establish formal contracts with lessees. Leases
can now be set for up to 10 years. These contracts would not be renewed
unless the lessee adopts appropriate land use practices. The contractor would
provide extension services and limited agricultural inputs to the lessees
(primarily) and then to the adjacent farmers of private lands.
7.        Methods
(a)  the contractor would initially identify and appraise
local social institutions (including farmer groups and
externally supported organizations), the nature and
availability of resources (financial, physical, human),
and the indigenous agricultural knowledge and technology
base;
(b)  the contractor would design an implementation strategy
which would strengthen and complement the local
institutions and technology, and result in the sustained
adoption and diffusion of initiated benefits beyond the
project life; and
(c)  the contractor would work through the identified local
institutions to provide a range of technical assistance
services including: agroforestry, soil conservation,
basic animal huobandry, crop diversification, crop
storage and marketing, water development, and
organizational skills.
8.        Proiect Guidelines:
(a)  the contractor would strengthen and encourage the formation of
peasant groups as described in para. 9 below. The contractor would
also develop local group linkages with external development



-127-
Appendix 1
Page 3 of 5
agencies providing other services not included in the project (such
as credit, water supply) to ensure that local institutions are not
dependent solely on the contractor for development assistance;
(b)  the contractor would focus on creating a local demand for
technologies and organizational skills rather than relying on
monetary or commodity incentives;
(c) the contractor would promote tree planting as an economic endeavor
in which the farmers are full owners of the trees and where the
trees serve as a cash crops and savingo banks to reduce local
vulnerability to cash contingencies;
(d) the contractor would promote agricultural development in a problem
solving context, enabling farmers and institutions to utilize a
problem identification, analysis and resolution strategy
formulation process;
(e) the contractor would encourage local innovation, the adaptation of
promoted technologies to individual farmer objectives and site
requirements, and the diffusion of interventions beyond the Reserve
area via social and economic linkages to other regionsa
(f) the contractor would collaborate with the Agroforestry Research
Section of the SRI to generate a body of locally specific
agricultural development information which would indicate
agricultural yields with and without interventions, and the
economic benefits of the agroforestry technologies utilized. The
Research Section would be responsible for the treatment and
publication of experiment results. On-farm, adaptive research
methods would be utilized;
(g) the contractor would identify and assist farmer groups/communities
interested in Improving their water supply in mobilizing funds
provided under the project for this purpose. Potential bene-
ficiaries would be expected to contribute a minimum of IOZ of the
total subproject cost in the form of free labor and/or local
materials; and
(h) the contractor would investigate and seek to develop local, pine
based micro-enterprises (such as furniture construction of pre-
commercially thinned polewood) which provide local employment and
generate revenues, and peasant collaborative ventures which seek
contracts from the SRI for forest management work.
9.        General Extension and Peasant Ornanization Guidelines.  The
contractor would generally follow the integrated resource conservation project
models provided by Helvetas/MPP, CARE/Leger, MCC, and SCF/Maissade.  The
contractor would be granted flexibility in the establishment of extension and



-128-
ANNEX 5
Appendix 1
Page 4 of 5
organization strategies. The following agroforestry extension and peasant
organization method is presented as a goneral guide and a suggested point of
departure for more detailed and site specific schemes. The contractor would
detail the specific methods in the project proposal.
(a) each program would initially conduct a rapid rural assessment to
identify salient local physical and social parameters including the
type and patterns of peasant groupings;
(b) programs would identify local candidates for extension positions
("animate") either by local popular election or by direct project
selection. These individuals would be trained in peasant
organization and/or agroforestry techniques;
(c)  extension agents would return to their respective communities to
conduct participatory local appraisals to identify and diagnose
local production, marketing and consumption patterns, and
constraints to development. Appraisals would also include the
discussion of preliminary local solutions and strategies for the
resolution of local problems;
(d) agents would identify existing local social groups (including labor
exchange groups, pre-established farmer groups, and community
associations) and provide education on basic organizational themes,
and methods to initiate collective economic activities. Technical
assistance to the groups would begin only after groups have
thoroughly examined local needs and requested project assistance.
Extension agents would not actively "create" new groups, but would
assist self-formed groups formalize. Other new groups would
spontaneously form in successive years as they witness the
activities and benefits gained through greater cooperation and
implementation of project techniques;
(e) groups or representatives from them would be given training in
agroforestry, soil conservation techniques rs well as other
agricultural training deemed appropriate by the project; and
(f) projects would continue to strengthen the groups (both pre-existent
and newly formed) encouraging their association and advancement to
more complex resource conservation and community development
projects. These participating groups would also be encouraged to
extend the messages beyond project boundaries.
10.       Contract OutDutss
(a) at least 1,750 farmers will have planted tree seedlings in
agroforectry configurations (this total can include repeat
planters);



-129-
MX 5,
Appendix I
Page 5 of S
(b) at least 1,000 farmers vill have initiated contour soil
conservation treatments on their land., and at least 500 will have
adopted crop diversification or improved crop storage techniques;
(c) at least 220,000 timber and 15,000 grafted fruit trees will have
been planted;
(d) at least 2,000 agricultural parcels will have been affected with
contour conservation treatments;
(e) at least 50 farmer groups which can Independently execute small
collective projects will be formed or improved through the
provision of organizational assistance;
(f) about 450 individual cisterns (serving two to three farm families
each), and about 4 collective cisterns (about 40,000 gallons each)
will be identified for construction in Year 2 and 3; and
(g) at least 15 local farmers will have received training in basic
animal husbandry techniques (Including nutrition and parasite
control), and provide regular technical assistance to their
respective communities.
Prolect Management
11.       The contractor would hold semi-annual internal evaluations and
program planning sessions. The contractor would also identify and monitor
indicators of benefit sustainability (e.g., levels of farmer innovation,
tecbnology adoption and diffusion rates, levels of farmer participation in
programs, ability of farmer groups to exploit external resources). The
contractor will be expected to be programmatically flexible, responding to the
sustainability indicators, and dynamic local needs. The contractor would
submit annual progrese reports, and an annual work plan and budget to the
Ministry of Agriculture on mutually agreed dates. A mobilization credit would
be advanced upon contract approval and regular withdrawal requests would be
made to the DRN. Disbursemento would be made, amounts again contingent upon
the negotiated contract agreement. An annual meeting between representatives
of the DRN and the contractor would be held prior to the authorization to
review progress and revise if necessary the stated contract guidelines and
outputs.
12.       Budaet:  The total budget for the initial three years would not
exceed US$800,000 including overhead costs and all contingencies.

OEMS=R  AND ENVIRONNEMEN    PRTCION PROJECT
agroforeatrv ExtensLgn La PLc Macava National Park
and Adiacent Buffer Areas
Terms of Reference
1.        Pungs  Towards the enhancement of local welfare and the control
of agricultural expansion, this project would increase agriculture and wood
production on authorized peasant holdings within the Pic Macaya National Park
and in the adjacent northern buffer area. The project would serve to catalyze
local capacity to sustain increases in local productivity beyond project life,
and simultaneously develop the capability of local institutions to manage
local development activities. The contractor would work independently, but
under the supervision of the Ministry of Agriculture's Directorate of Natural
Resources (DRN) and ito Envlronmental Protection Service (SPE). The
contractor would complement the ongoing Pic Macaya Biosphere Reserve effort
coordinated by the University of Florida.
2.        Duration:  The project would be funded for four years, beginning
1992 and terminating 1996.  The NGO contract would be initially for two years,
extendable to four years, subject to the results of the mid-term evalu.Aon of
the entire Forestry and Environmental Protection Project.
3.        Location:  The Pic Macaya National Park is located 195 km southwest
of Port-au-Prince and 36 km north of Les Cayes on the Nassif de la Hotte. The
"core" preserved area totals 5,500 ha and the surrounding "buffer zone"
15,000. Target communities include Duchiti and Beaumont. It is suggested
that the contractor base its field operations in the community of Des
Barriers. The contractor would collaborate with SPE field teams which are
charged with Park delineation, inventory and protection and the regulation of
farmlands within the Park.
4.        Problem Statements  The Pic Macaya National Park area contains
important and unique vestiges of high altitude wildlife and vegetation species
and habitat. Agricultural encroachment into the Park has threatened genetic
diversity and resulted in the elimination of some species. Important
watershed areas are also damaged causing significant damage downstream.
Farmers in the adjacent areas are faced with unstable and declining yields,
use few agricultural inputs or substantial conservationist technique. Farmers
are also largely unorganized, engage in little community development activity,
and have limited ability to exploit external development resources for local
benefit. Local residents have also received little legal, direct benefit from
the Park or technical assistance and development assiatance from the
Government.

FORESXRY AND ENVIRONMENTAl  PRECTCION PROJECT
Aaroforestrv,Production In the Belle hAse
Reaion of Southeast Haiti
1.        Purnoses  The purpose of this project is to increase agriculture
and wood production on peasant lands to enhance local welfare and decrease the
rate of agricultural expansion. The project will serve to catalyze local
capacity to sustain increases in local productivity beyond project life. The
contractor would work under the supervision of the Ministry of Agriculture's
Directorate of Natural Resources (DRN) and its Forestry Resource Service
(SRI).
2.        Duration:  The project would be implemented for four years,
beginning in 1992 and terminating 1996. The NGO contract would be initially
for two years, extendable to four years, subject to the results of the mid-
term evaluation of the entire Forestry and Environmental Protection Project.
3.        Location   The project would target the upper elevations of the
Belle Anse Region in southeast Haiti, south of the La Visite National Park and
Pine Forest Preserves, east of the village of Seguin and west of the town of
Thiotte.
4.        Problem Statements  The area targeted Is mountainous, ranging from
300 to 2,000 m in elevation with 600 to 1,400 mm of annual rainfall. Most of
the land is cropped annually with little or no conservationist measures or
agricultural inputs utilized. Declining agricultural yields and land sub-
division cause the expansion of agricultural activity to new, more fragile
lands. These new lands are frequently located in or near the protected Pine
Forest Reserve or La Visite National Park.
S.        Method:
(a) the contractor would initially identify and appraise local
institutions, the nature and availability of resources, and the
indigenous agriculture knowledge and technology base;
(b) the contractor would design and implementation strategy which would
strengthen and complement local institutions and technology and
result in the sustained improvement and diffusion of initiated
benefits beyond project life;
(c) the contractor would support the delivery of tree seedlings and
other perennial germplasm and technical assistance in agroforestry
to local farmers. The project would focus on the promotion of two
principal technologies; tree planting in agroforestry



-141-
Appendix 4
Page 2 of 3
configurations (Cog., boundary, hom, garden., contour), and the
iv'tallation of contour soil conservation hedgerow.; and
(d) to assure continued adoption and diffusion of agroforestry
technologies and benefits beyond GO intervention, the contractor
would encourage local propagation of plant materials with
appropriate, low coat methods (e.g., group and household nurseries,
direct seeding, and wildling transplantation).
6.        Project Guidelinegs
(a) the contractor would use an education approach, focusing on
creating a local demand for technologies rather than relying on
monetary or commodity incentives for adoptiont
(b) the contractor would promote tree planting as an economic endeavor
in which the farmers are full owners of the trees and where the
trees serve as a cash crops and savings banks to reduce local
vulnerability to cash contingenciest and
(c) the contractor would train eand utilize local residents as extension
agents and employ incentive packages which assure that the agents
would continue to live and passively promote the agroforestry
interventions beyond project termination.
7.        Proiect Outnut&:
(a) at least 12,000 farmers will have planted project delivered tree
seedlings (this total can include repeat planters);
(b) at least 2,000 farmers will have propagated tree seedlings without
project subsidy (this sum can be included in the 12,000 total
planter target);
(c) at least 2,820 farmers will have installed contour conservation
techniques; and
(d) at least 2,500,000 trees will have been planted by farmers on their
private parcels.
Prolect Manaaement
8.        The contractor would hold semi-annual internal evaluation and
program planning sessions. The contractor would also identify and monitur
indicators of benefit sustainability (e.g., levels of farmer innovation,
technology adoption and diffusion rates, levels of farmer participation in
programs). The contractor will be expected to be programatically flexible,
responding to the sustainability indicators, and dynamic local needs. The
contractor would submit annual progress reports, end an annual work plan and
budget to the Ministry of Agriculture on mutually agreed dates. A

EQOESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
Environmental Protection Servlo
I. Present Situati2o
1.        Due to the great variation in altitude, rainfall, soil type, and a
70 million year history of geographical separation, Haiti is characterized by
a large diversity of plant and animal species and an unusually high number of
important ecologic sites. Consequently, Haiti possesses a high number of
endemic plant and animal species.
2.        The intense exploitation of the land and vegetation resources since
the arrival of humans in Haiti 7,000 years ago has destroyed most natural
habitats and permanently altered much of the Haitian landscape. Vestiges of
the original forest ecosystems exist in only a few areas and almost all of
their associated plant and animal species are now threatened. Because Haiti
is an island, when the habitats are destroyed there cannot be any
recolonization of its fragile ecosystems by extirpated plant and animal
species because there are no adjacent areas to serve as refuges. Many species
disappear forever with the destruction of their particular ecosystems. A
large number of species are now classified as "rare" or "endangered" by
international conservation organizations. An unknown number of species have
already become extinct, and in especially vulnerable groups, such as land
mAimnals, 89Z of all known species are now extinct.
3.        The most recent aerial inventory of vegetation and land use of the
entire country was completed in 1978, and no current information is available
to indicate the status of the remainiug forest resources, ecosystems and
species of the country except for the regions of the national parks at Pic
Macaya and Horne La Visite that were established by Presidential decree in
April 1983. As a result, there is a paucity of information on the status of
natural resources in the country, and almost no information as to which
ecosystems are healthy and capable of supporting commercial activities such as
sustainable forestry or intensive fisheries, and which ecosystems are severely
damaged and in need of immediate protection and long-term management. There
is no reliable information on productivity, trends, and total amount of forest
products which is important to know when addressing the country's fuelwood
problems.
4.        In the 1980's several agencies initiated programs aimed at
protecting Haiti's forest ecosystems and promoting biodiversity. Though the
legal and planning framework has been written for the protection of Pic Macaya
and Morn. La Visits National Parks, major protection efforts have been slow to
be initiated (and have been nearly ineffective) because sufficient funding has



-144-
ANhI A
Page 2 of 14
not been available. Confusion over which govearmental organization is
responsible for the national parks has also complicated the estdbleb!nnt of
an effective stewardship program. The original 1983 Decree establishing the
national parka at La 7isite and Pic Macaya was not specific as to which GOR
organization was to take primary responsibility for the administration of the
parks. USAID developed a program with the Institut de Sauvegarde du
Patrimoine National (ISPAN), and provided ISPAN with a grant to develop a
management plan for the national parks at La Vilito and Macaya. However,
ISPAN did not develop the infrastructure necessary to manage the parks on a
long-term basis, and has not been directly involved with the management of the
national parks at La Visits and Pic Nacaya since 1988. In 1988, ISPAN
contacted the Service of Forest Resources (SRP) by letter and asked it SRP
would pay for some national park related expenses at La Visite, including
salaries for the park guard&. The SRI agreed to do this, and to assume the
responsibility for the management of the parks. Therefore, while the
administrative status of the national parks is vague, it is clear that DRN is
legally responsible for the parks, and that ISPAN has notified SRI that it
wishes for It to assume responsibility for the park at La Visite. SRI has
done this. ISPAN currently lacks any funding for the administration of the
national "natural" parkes vor does it have any staff assigned to these
national parks, although it continues to manage the national historic parks of
Haiti (especially the 2,200 ha Parc National Historique la Citadelle
established in 1968).
5.        In this document a national park is a "preserve", in which the
primary objective is the management of the area for the preservation of
biological diversity. A "reserve" is a forest area in which the primary
objective is the management of the area for the harvest of forest resources.
A "biosphere reserve" is a combination of both objectives, with the activities
designated by "zones". In a typical biosphere reserve the core zones, which
emphasize preservation, are found mostly within a national park. The core
zone is surrounded by other zones including: (i) rehabilitation zones;
(ii) forestry zones; (III) agroforestry zones; (iv) agricultural zones;
(v) multiple use zonesl and (vi) research zones. Active interventions such as
lease management and the utilization of extension services are appropriate in
zones other than the core zone. The term "national parks" within Haiti is
confusing because of the presence of national historic parks managed by ISPAN,
and national "natural" parks managed by MARNDR. In this document whenever the
term "national park" is used it refers only to the national "natural" parks
under the administrative control of MARNDR.
6.        The University of Florida Macaya Biosphere Reserve Project (UF-
MBRP) has worked as a NGO contractor of USAID in the buffer zone south and
west of Pic Macaya National Park, and in the southern half of the park itself.
This project was initiated on October 1, 1987, and is expected to continue
receiving fu..ing from USAID through August 1993. However, political
guidelines of USAID have prevented the UP-MBRP from working in association
with the Government of Haiti (GOH) for most of the life of the project. The
activities of the UF-MBRP includet (i) sociocultural inventories of the
southern district of the park and adjacent districts (buffer zone);



-145-
AIDIA
Page 3 of 14
(ii) zoning the southern regions of the park and buffer zone; (Lit) analysis
of land use trends; and (iv) development of an agricultural and agroforestry
extension progrtn in the southern Macaya region. Since the GOB has the
administrative responsibility for *1I state lando, and since Parc National Pic
Macaya is on public domain state lands under the administrative control of
MARNDR, the management of the Ple Macaya Project has been complex (and
flawed). The situation is further complicated by the mission of the USAID
project, which defines the geographical limits of its Targeted Watershed
Management Project as south of Pic Macaya. The existing UP-MBRP, therefore,
is technically only concerned with half of the existing national park and
associated buffer zone.
II. The Environmental Protection Service
7.        The Service de la Protection de l'Enlvironnoment (SPE) was first
created by Administrative order in 1984, and legally recognized by the
"Conseil National de Couvernement" Decree of 1987. The intention of DRN was
to place the supervision of the parks under the SPE, but this was not done
earlier because of the lack of resources available to the SPE. For example,
it was the SRP that assumed responsibility for the park at La Visite in 1988
instead of SPE because SRI had funds available and SPE did not.
S.        The SPE in 1990 had three professional staff (including the chief)
who were assigned to two sections:
(a) Section on Research and Inventories.
(b) Section on Preservation and National Parks.
The project would enhance these existing two sections, and add one additional
section:
(c)  Section on Cartography and Information Services.
The new section would be responsible for centralizing the data collected by
the inventories, and the information collected by the other two sections, and
making it available in the form of data bases and Geographic Information
Systems (GIS).
9.        One additional section of the SPE existed on paper (Section on the
Prevention of Pollution), but did not have any staff members assigned to it in
1990. This section would not be enhanced as part of the project.
10.       Although two of SPE's three professional staff members have
training in conservation and management of natural resources, the SPE does not
have the financial resources and staff number necessary to initiate and
complete an inventory of Haiti's fragile ecosystems, develop the decision
making skills necessary to manage these fragile ecosystems, and supervise
staff that would be assigned to remote locations in the existing national

parks. As a reoult, SPE has not yet been able to assume full responsibility
for the management of the existing national parks. It is important,
therefore, that SPE be strengthened as a Servi:e so that it has the resources
to assume all of its assigned duties in managing parks and protecting the
onvironment.

(b)  Inventorv Forest Resources on a National Scale.  Forest areas would

resources, remaining areas of forest cover, the total available
forest biomass, end the rate of deforestation.
(c) Identify New Natiolg ParXka and Forest Reserves. Data gathered by
the land use survey and the inventory of forest resources would be
used to identify candidate ecosystems for a new national park, as
well as identify forests which should be designated as forest
reserves to be managed by the SRF.
(d)  MNnage the Two Existing National Parks end Create One New National
"ark.  SPE would assume lamediate responsibility for the management
of the existing national parks at Horne La Visite and Pic Macaya,
and would complete the activities necessary to add one additional
national park to the system by year four of the project.
(e) Preserve Endgnagred Plant an Animal Snecies. SPE would compile
data on threatened, rare, and endangered species, and establish a
program to protect these species.
(f)  getablish and Manaae Data Bases on Renewable Natural Resources.
SIB would enter the data from baseline sociocultural and
