IV. Prolect Components
13.       Details of the project activities that would be undertaken by the
SPE are as follows:
A.  National Land Use Assessment
14.       Satellite imagery would be utilized to periodically monitor country
wide land use trends, and the distribution of ecosystems. The Landsat (EOSAT)
Thematic Mapper (TM) as well as the SPOT system will be used. The Landsat
system has a greater spectral range and provides the required information at
lower costs (TM: US$.105/kma, SPOT: US$.64/ke?). The Imagery from EOSAT would
be hard copy ls50,000 imagery prepared from a 3-band composite that would
emphasize vegetative cover. This would provide a fairly detailed inventory of
land use patterns, and land resources. Manual interpretation of these images
would includos "geocoding" the images, and entering data on urban areas, barren
ground, brush lands, major rivers, coastal wetlands, roads, forest cover
(three crown density measures), sand, agricultural areas, water, wetlands, and
rural vAl1ages. Because the topography in Haiti is so steep, and the area of
the country is relatively small, resolution becomes an important
consideration. The SPOT system provides higher resolution, and it therefore
will be used (along with aerial photography) for more detailed analyses. The
ground truthing for the analysis of the satellite images will be conducted by
the SPE inventory team. The local SPE staff would be trained in
interpretation and validation with the assistance from a short-term
consultant.
15.       Flight surveys (aerial pnotography) would be utilized to determine
local land use patterns, the locations of specific renewable natural
resources, and to document activities in areas of special concern such as core
zones in national parks and inventory areas. Some aerial photographs exist,
such as the outdated photographs in Geodetic Institute, and the photographs of
the Pine Forest Reserve obtained by the SRF which include a small section of
the national park at La Visite. These existing photographs would not be
recant enough, nor cover are-s of primary concern by the SPE (La Visite,
Macaya, Environmental Profile Areas). Additional aerial photographs and
flight surveys will be contracted by SPE to fill in the gaps in the available
data, and to provide additional data needed to identify candidate areas, study
forest resources and document land use trends. These aerial photographs would



-149-
Page 7 of 14
be interpreted initially by the firm as part of the contract, but later by the
staff of the SPE once they have the proper training to do so.
D. Forest ResourceJ Inventorv
16.       Forst Attributes.  The forest types delineated on the satellite
images would be subsampled to produce more specific information on the forest
resources (crown cover, species, density, and biomass volumes). An aerial
survey and ground inventory of the chosen forest types would provide data on
forest cover and density attributes. $PE would produce biomass tables for
every forest class. Biomass and area totals for all classes would be summed
to represent total biomass availability.
17.       Patterns and Nature of Wood Harvestint and Utilization.  An
essential question for forestry planners in Haiti for each forest type is -
What level and for what products is current wood harvesting sustainable? Data
generated by aerial photographs and satellite images (land use and forest
type) provide valuable information on proper forest classifications. Biomass
tables for each forest substratum provide information on potential wood
availability. This information alone, however, presents an inaccurate picture
of actual wood use, wood production, and true trends in the availability of
forest resources, and additional sociocultural inventories would therefore be
carried out under the project (para. 18).
18.   . ociocultural Inventories of Forest Resource Utilization.
Sociocultural surveys in selected regions would be used to inform planners of
the level at which wood harvesting is sustainable by documenting rates of wood
production, patterns of harvesting and utilization, all of which are
geographically and forest type specific. Sociocultural surveys of wood use
would be carried out with the assistance of short-term consultants for each
forest stratum to describe local wood consumption patterns; specifying the
types, amounts, and purposes of wood utilized, and the use of other tree
products and utilizations (shade, forage, medicinal, etc.). The sociocultural
information would help planners document the causes and rates of forest
depletion and degradation. Upon the completion of the inventory by SPE, SRF
staff would continue a more detailed analysis of national wood production and
consumption patterns, and initiate planning activities for future forestry
investments.
19.       Lont-Term Forest Inventories on Observation Plots (Ecosystem
Monitoring). Permanent plots would be installed in ecologically
representative sites in each of the forest reserves and national parks. These
would be designated as "research zones". The goal is to establish at least
one research zone of 100 ha in each national park and forest reserve. The
research zones will be fenced and/or guarded to protect them from illegal
exploitation. Data on species composition, growth, natural succession, and
the propagation of indigenous species would be gathered and monitored.



-150-
Page 8 of 14
C. Identification of a New NAtional Park and Additional forest Reserves
20.       SPE would identify approximately 30,000 ha that would be suitable
as candidate areas for the new national park to be established in the fourth
year of the project, and as candidate areas for future forest reserves. The
method of selection and establishment of these areas is described in detail in
para. 23. The major ecosystems to be investigated are as follows:
(a) Manarovess Because of their inter-tidal location, mangrove
ecosystems play an essential role in the reproductive cycles of
numerous aquatic species, and provide habitat for economically
important species of crustaceans. In addition threatened species
such as the Greater Flamingo and West Indian manatee occur in bays
and lagoons associated with mangrove habitats. Although a majority
of the original mangrove stands have been eliminated, forests
remain in the bays of Acul and Caracol (near Cap-Haltian), the
Cayes region (Aquin Bay, Ile & Vache), the north coast of the
southern peninsula (BaradAres and Corail). The most substantial
remaining forest is in the Artibonite River Delta (south of
Gonaives and west of l'Estare). Though mangrove forests are within
the public domain, rights of access and harvest are often allocated
between private individuals. Preliminary studies indicate that the
Caracol and Artibonite areas are the best candidates for protection
because they have the largest concentrations of threatened and
endangered species. Preliminary plans by SPE indicate that this
ecosystem has the highest priority as a candidate for the new
national park.
(b) Broadleaf Forest: Though much of Haiti was originally covered with
this forest type, most of the broadleaf stands have been converted
to coffee plantations and multi-purpose agricultural plots.
Important broadleaf forests remain south of the tvwn of Borgne, on
the Morne Brigand and Boucan Michel and west to the headwaters of
the Anse-&-Foleur watershed and in the Pic Macaya area. Some of
these are on state land, especially west of Pic Hacaya, and could
be attached to the Macaya Biosphere Reserve as part of Parc
National Pic Macaya.
(c) Semi-arid Forest: This forest community, which is characterized by
the dominance of Prosoiis, has historically been the source of most
of the charcoal produced in Haiti, and vast areas of semi-arid
forest have become degraded through over-harvesting. Remnants of
natural stands remain on the edges of the Etang SaumAtre (in the
Cul-de-Sac plain), Grand Gosier (southeast Haiti) and near Cates de
Fer (southern coast of Haiti), and on the western end of Ile de la
Gonave. All of these lands are within the public domain.
(d)  Arid-land scrub:  This ecosystem is characterized by cactus,
succulents and thorny shrubs. The zone which most characterizes



-151-
Page 9 of 14
this vegetation type Is found along the coast between the tomns of
Anse Rouge and Gonalves (Savane Desol6e). The cactus and scrub
resources are rapidly being depleted for charcoal and fuel uses.
This land is apparently within the public domain.
21.       gociocultural Inventory of Candidate Areas.  SPE staff would carry
out the sociocultural inventory of each candidate area using the Rapid Rural
Assessment method. The Information to be gatherad would include:
(i) demographics of the population living within and adjacent to the proposed
reserve or preserve; (li) the nature of the use of natural resources;
(iii) patterns of access and authority on the site; (iv) land tenure
arrangements; (v) ownership of the resources; (vi) the economic benefits
accrued by user populations; and (vii) descriptions of local agricultural
strategies. The inventory would also identify existing or potential conflicts
that might result from management interventions, and recommend alternative
methods that should be used to mitigate or avoid conflict, maintain user
welfare, and integrate users and local residents Into the plan for the
protection of the ecosystem. A social science specialist would assist in
these aspects of the sociocultural inventory as a short-term consultant.
22.       Environmentgl Profiles of Candidate Areas.  The purpose of the
environmental profile would be to establish baseline environmental Information
on each area that is being considered as the future national park, or as a
forest reserve. Each profile would describe the major biotic and abiotic
features, their status, and recommend measures to preserve biological
diversity.
23.       Creation of a Ne  National Park.  The procedure for establishing
the new national park and future forest reserves would be as follows: (i) SPE
would conduct intensive sociocultural surveys and environmental profiles to
determine the suitability of each candidate area (para. 21-22); (ii) SPE would
prepare a document describing and summarizing the information on forest cover,
forestry potential, natural resource components, status of important endemic
flora and fauna, existing patterns of human occupancy and land use, and land
tenure characteristics for each candidate area. The document would include a
recommendation as to the best land use strategy for each candidate area (i.e.,
national park or forest reserve); (Iii) the Director of DRN would review the
recommendatins with SRP and SPE and decide which recommendations would be
implemented under the project. SRF would manage areas designated as forest
reserves. SPE would manage all national parks; (iv) the designation of the
area as an official national park would occur after MARNDR prepares a draft
Decree for consideration by the Council of Ministers. The Decree would
identify which sites would be transferred Into the public domain as national
parks or forest reserves under MUhDR. Following the approval of the Decree
by the Council, it would be published in La Moniteur and become official; and
(v) SPE would arrange for the delimitation of the designated national park
area, and the legal transfer of the ourveyed area under the jurisdiction of
MAUDR.



-152-
Page 10 of 14
D. &anSement of National Parke
24.       Haiti's existing natural national parka and forest reserves ares
Pare National Pic Macaya (5,500 ha), Pare National La Visite (3,000 ha) and
the Pine Forest Reserve (27,871 ha), 8P would manage the two national parks
(which are "preserves"), while the management of the Pine Forest Reserve
(which is a multi-use "reserve") would remain under SRI. The following
activities would be implementod by aPE under the project for each of the two
existing national parka, and for the one additional national park that would
be established by year four.
25.       Delimitation of National Park..  1PE would contract the Direction
Generale des Impoto (DGI) for a survey to delimit each national park with
funding provided under the project. A technician from SPE would accompany the
DGI survey team to advise the DOI as to where the boundary should be located
relative to existing natural and man-made features of the area (i e., avoid
problems of including existing farms or excluding ecologically Important
features). SPE would offer to provide DWI survey team with tents, camping
equipment, and logistical support while the team is in the field. SRF would
be responsible for making arrangements to survey and delineate forest
reserves.
26.       Demarcation of Rational Park.  SPE work teams, under the
supervision of the park supervisor, would place concrete monuments and
metallic signs at regular intervals along the perimeter of each national park.
Signs would be posted at the junctions of major trails and access roads.
Three guard houses would be built In each national park to shelter the park
rangers, and provide a symbolic presence for the authority of SF3 in the
management of the parka. A total of 18 km of fences would be erected in
vulnerable or exposed areas to protect core and research zones. Part of the
process of demarcation would also be the establishment of clearly marked land
use zones where management activities will be undertaken by SF. The
boundaries of zones would be marked by paint on trees or wooden posts.
27.       Supervislon of the National Parks.  For each park an agronomist
would be assigned as the park supervisor. The park supervisor would be
responsible for all activities in the park, and would also supervise the
activities of the NGO Agroforestry Extension project In the area (para. 33).
A forest technician would be appointed as the supervisor of the park rangers.
This technician would have received training in ecology, flora and fauna, and
park management in order to qualify him to supervise the activities of the
rangers.
28.       Protection of the Resources of the National Parks.  SPE would
employ park rangers who would be carefully selected, appropriately trained by
EMAP (Annex 7), regularly paid, and consistently supervised. These conditions
would be similar to those of the forest rangers of SRP. The park rangers
would patrol the lands within the park boundaries, control the entrance and
exit of vehicles and pedestrian traffic, and encourage the compliance with
park regulations, laws, and zoning guidelines. Park rangers would undertake



-153-
6M  6
Page 11 of 14
other protective services as designated by the supervisors of each national
park, and would stay in regular contact with their supervisors via radio-
communications. At the completion of the project a total of 32 park rangers
would be employed.
29.       Limitation of Access Roads ag a Hanaaement Tool. Access to the
parks via wvll developed and direct roads would be discouraged during the
project in order to provide an extra measure of protection for the parks until
an adequate protective infrastructure is established. The national parks have
potential as tourist attractions, especially La Visite. However, access roads
are necessary before this potential can be realized. The existing road from
Port-au-Prince to Morne La Visits provides the most direct access to the park,
however, it has been impassible to vehicles since 1988. SPE has determined
that this road should not be rebuilt during the period of the project, and
that it is necessary to keep this road closed to vehicles until the park
infrastructure is well established. Otherwise, irreparable harm could be done
by illegal wood harvesters and transient cultivators who make seasonal
gardens. SPE would rebuild the access road from the south of the park at La
Visits because access via the town of Seguin can be controlled, and would
rebuild the service roads through the park and to Unit II of the Pine Forest
Reserve at Morne Rouge. SPE would not build a major access road into the
north side of Parc National Pic Macaya because of the same concerns. The
strategy is to develop the infrastructure of each national park before
developing easy access to the parks.
30.       Manaaeme't of "Occubied" Lands within Park Boundaries.  One of the
major problems faced by SPE is the presence of farms within the boundaries of
the national parks. Some of the problems created by the presence of farms in
the parks aret (i) farmers who expand existing gardens into new areas;
(ii) livestock which are grazed in inappropriate areas; (iii) slash and burn
garden plots in remote areas; and (iv) the introduction of dogs, cats, rats,
and mongoose, all of which follow humans into a region and do great harm to
endemic animals. These problems are most significant at La Visits National
Park, where farmers living within the park were given a letter from MARNDR in
1983 notifying them that they were to leave the park by 1985. Farmers in
La Visits Park have not paid leases on the land they occupy since that date.
The relocation of these farmers was not carried out because they were notified
that they would receive land outside the park in exchange for the land they
currently occupied. Under the project, SPE would resolve this problem as
follows. Leases would not be collected from any farmers who have already
received a relocation notice. The land within the boundaries of each park
would be zoned for its proper land use at the earliest possible date. Some
lands within the boundaries of the parks would be determined to be suitable
for agriculture, and would be zoned as "agricultural zones" or "multiple use
zones". Peasants who live and farm in these areas would be allowed to remain
there. In areas within the national parks which are zoned for preservation
(i.e, "core zones" and "research zones"), peasants would receive a letter
similar to the one that was used by MARNDR at La Visite notifying them that
they would be relocated (para. 30). In the special case where a farmer living
within the boundaries of the La Visits Park National, he received an eviction



-154-
AXLX 6
Page 12 of 14
notice in 1983, but the land he occupies is zoned for agriculture by SPE under
this project, then the eviction notice would be voided and a new lease
agreement written using the above guidelines.
31.       Relocation of Peasants frcm Core Zonoe.  It is important that the
letter of eviction is accompanied by a detailed proposal for compensation for
the lose of the lcnd(s) that is currently occupied. SPE favors paying the
peasants a one-time payment for "damages", and thus providing them with the
means to lease lands at another location. Payments will only be made to
farmers vho can show documentation that they were holders of leases for the
land, or occupied the site for at least five years in order to prevent the
public from perceiving that they can gain land and money by occupying national
park lands, as has been the case in the national parks program in the
Dominican Republic.
32.       Manaaement of Leases.  In areas of the national parks zoned for
agriculture, agroforestry, or multiple use, land leases would be used by SPE
as a management tool. Leases would be written by SPE with the assistance of
legal counsel, and would define which land use activities are allowed and
which not. Leases would initially be for periods of one year, and would not
commit SPE to allowe the peasants to lease the land indefinitely. If a farmer
is found to occupy land in an area that is zoned for agriculture,
agroforestry, or multiple use activities, and if the park supervisor feels
that the presence of the farmer does not compromise the management plan for
the park, a long-term lease would be established. Long- term leases would
encourage farmers to invest into their holdings and take advantage of the
extension services provided by the project. These long-term leases would be
for ten years, but would give SPE the authority to cancel the lease annually
if the land use conditions of the lease were violated by the farmer (Annex 3,
para. 7). The superviaor of each park would be responsible for the
administration of the lease program in that park.
33.       Lease Fees.  There are no legal guidelines within MARNDR as to
where money collected from leases on MARNDR land should be deposited.
Revenues from fines and the sale of contraband forest are deposited in the
Fund for Reforestation (1972 law) that is being administered by SRF. The fees
collected from leases and from fines in the national parks would also be
deposited in the Reforestation Fund. These funds would be used in accordance
with the existing law, and would be handled in the same way that leases are to
be handled in the Pine Forest Reserve (Annex 3, para. 15).
34.       Supervision of the Agroforestry Proiects Surroundina Each National
Lark. DRN would contract with NGO's to provide agroforestry extension in the
national parks and in the buffer zones surrounding the national parks at La
Visite and Pic Macaya (Annex 5). In the case of Pic Nacaya, extension
activities would be initiated in the area north of the park, since the UF-MBR
Project is already working in a similar capacity in the buffer zone South of
the park. The objective of the agroforestry extension contract is to provide
agricultural extension services to peasants living near the park to reduce the
danger of environmental degradation within the park, and to increase local



-155-
MM 6
Page 13 of 14
standards of living. NOO'. would also operate agricultural extension services
to design recommendations for agroforestry and soil conservation technique.
that would be used to establish conditions for leases (Annex 3, para. 7), and
to verify that conservation treatments on leased lands have taken place. SPE
would supervise the agroforestry extension activities through the supervisor
of each national park (resident agronomist).
35.       Biosphere Reserme Plan.  SPE would develop an extended management
plan for both the park and buffer zone as the first step in expanding the UP-
HBR Management Plan to the north slope of Pic Macaya. In developing this
plan, SPE would modify the management plan developed by the University of
Florida under a contract with USAID for the national park at Pic Macaya to
include: (i) information on biosphere reserve land use zones; (1i) activities
that would be appropriate. for the surrounding buffer zonel and (iii) the
reconmendations contained in the existing UF-MBR Management Plan.
E. Preservation of Endanaered Species
36.         The preservation of biological diversity is difficult in Haiti
because of the extent of deforestation that has already occurred, and special
efforts would need to be undertaken to preserve some of the country's most
endangered plant and animal species. SPE staff would propagate and grow
endangered species of the lowland endemic plants such as Attalea crassipatha,
Geonoma oxicar2a, and Zombia antAllarium at the HARDR owned Levy Farm near
Camp Perrin. A similar program for upland species such as Junilerus eckmanii
would be undertaken in conjunction with SRP at the nursery in the Pine Forest
Reserve. SPE would develop special programs to improve the habitat for
threatened species by including these activities in the management plan for
the existing national parks (for example providing nest boxes for birds who
normally nest in cavities in mature trees).
37.         SPE would also protect endangered species and preserve biological
diversity by: (i) developing "recovery plans" for endangered species and
species of special concern, and include the activities and recommendations
contained in these recovery plans in the management plans for each national
park and forest reserve; (ii) administrating current wildlife regulations and
laws by requiring permits and licenses to hunt, collect plants and animals,
and export any native species of plant or animal; (iii) drafting additional
legislation to protect the native flora, fauna, and threatened habitats of the
country; and (iv) seeking international assistance from organizations, such
as, the International Office of the Nature Conservancy, or Wildlife
International, to purchase special parcels of threatened ecosystems which
serve as habitat for rare, threatened, or endangered plants and animals.
V. Implementation
38.         One of the requirements of the project is to have a sufficient
number of agroforestry technicians on the staff of SPE to carry objectives of
the project. Since there are not enough agroforestry technicians currently
available in Haiti to staff the project, agroforestry technicians will need to



-156-
Page 14 of 14
be trained at EMAP. SPE will have an iediate need to hire two agroforestry
technicians during the first year of the project to work on the inventory
team. These technicians are currently available. There are not enough
agroforestry technicians available to be assigned to the existing national
parks and to Levy Farm, since the first graduating class from EMAP will not be
available until the third year of the project. At that time SPE will hire
eight agroforeetry technicians (La Visito - 21 Macaya - 41 Levy Farm - 1;
Inventory Team - 1). In the fourth year of the project an additional national
park will be established by SPE, and two additional agroforestry technicians
vill be hired (for a total of 12 for SPE In the project). During the first
two years of the project, while the agroforestry technicians are being trained
by EMAP, the agronomists assigned to each national park and to Levy Farm will
serve in an expanded role that will include supervising the park rangers and
plant propagators. The expanded duties of the supervisors would also provide
the opportunity to increase their experiences at all levels in the operation
of the national parks, and increase their abilities to supervise and manage
their respective parks.
39.         Other phasing that will occur in the project are as follows.  All
activities at Parc La Visite will begin the first year of the project, but only
half the guards will be hired at Parc Macaya. In year two of the project all of
the guards will be hired at Parc Macaya. In year three of the project all of the
agroforestry technicians will be hired for the existing parks, and the staff of
each park and Levy Farm will be operating at full staff levels (see above). In
year four of the project one additional national park will be added to SPE system
of protected areas, and a full staff will be hired. The agronomist that will
supervise this park will have been employed by the SPE since the first year of
the project, and will have previously been assigned to the Levy Farm. He/she
will have three years of SPE experience and a knowledge of management techniques
based on previous experiences with the national parks program, when he/she
becomes supervisor of the new national park

after the mid-term review of the project.

throughout Haiti.

FORES=R- AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
Tralninoc
Current Trainina Situation
1.          The University of Haiti does not offer training sequences in
forestry, wildlife conservation and natural resource management. Only three
Haitian professionals, all agroinomists, had received graduate training in
forestry outside of Haiti. During the implementation of the Pilot Forestry
Project (Credit 1257-HA), implemented between 1983 and 1989, the following
training activities were undertaxen:
(a)   Eight graduates of the Facult6 d'Agriculture et de Medecine
Veterinaire (FAmW) received graduate training in forestry at the
Ecole Nationale du Genie Rural, des Eaux et Forets (ENGREF) in
Montpellier (France). Two students were awarded master's degrees
after two years. The other six students lacked the necessary
qualifications to continue beyond the first year of study. In
retrospect, it also appears that the ENGREF program was not well
adapted to the needs of Haitian students or the Pilot Forestry
Project.
(b)   The Pilot Forestry Project introduced an agroforestry curriculum
at a technical school built with Canadian aseistance, the Ecole
Moyenne d'Agroforesterie (EMAF). Under the pilot project the EMAF
carried out three two-year cycles of training and produced 60
graduates. Most graduates were employed by the project and the
Forest service (SRF). Some have been employed by NGOs working in
agroforestry extension. In terms of continuing employment in
forestry, there has been an overall attrition rate of 20% among
the 60 EMAF graduates.  EMAF has made an important contribution to
human resource development in Haitit however, evaluation of the
EMAF program suggests certain areas in need of strengthening.
Despite its name, EMAF stressed classical forestry traLning over
agroforestry. It also emphasized theory rather than practical
skills. In general, curriculum development at EMAF was not based
on detailed aossesment of training needs in the field. As a
training lnstitution, EMAF has not had the benefit of a fulltime
core faculty. Therefore, it has been overly dependent on adjunct
professors drawn primarily from FAMV.



-166-
ANNEX 7
Page 2 of 6
2.          Since its inception as an agroforestr!, training center, the EMAF
has relied on external funding to operate its program. The EMAF has a close
working relationship with the SRF which has employed most EMAF graduates. The
EMAF is one of seven technical training centers of the agricultural ministry.
These training centers operate under the authority of the Direction de
Formation et de Perfectionnement des Cadres (DFPC), a training department
created by the MARNDR Organic Law of 1987. The EMAF retained its special
relationship to SRF even after the DFPC was created. Unlike other
"directions" in the ministry, the DFPC is a semi-autonomous support service.
The DFPC Director reports to the MARNDR Director-General. In 1989/90 the EMAF
and other DFPC training centers were closed due to the budgetary crisis in
government, and the cutoff of funding by external donors.
Proiect-Training obiectives
3.          The project's training strategy would be directly linked to the
programmatic requirements of the Forest Service (SRF) and the Environmental
Protection Service (SPE) of the Department of Natural Resources. Training
programs would be carefully adapted to the local context. They would be based
on detailed needs assessment of SRF and SPE and their project related
activities including management ot national parks and forest reserves. EMAF
would develop curricula and training materials in relation to the specific
skills required to carry out work assignments in the field. EMAF training
activities would balance theoretical knowledge with practical field experience
as an integral feature of its curriculum.
4.          In general, the training undertaken by the project would include a
mix of short- and long-term training activities. It would stress in-country
training and the development of Haiti's long term training capabilities,
including in-service staff training on an ongoing basis. Training would also
include retraining of selected NARNDR staff already in post, to qualify them
for reassignment to positions under the project. The training program would
result in a specialized cadre of human resources with expertise in forestry
ecosystems, small farm systems, agroforestry, wildlife management and
preservation of biodiversity. The primary targets for training would be the

Imvrovement of the Woodfuels Taxation  svstem
9.          Tree cutting and transport of wood and charcoal is subject to a
permit that is being issued by the forestry agents of MARNDR's field offices.
Transport of woodfuel is, in addition, subject to a transport tax. A tax of
G 1.0 is to be collected on each bag (38 kg) of charcoal transported from the
production site to the sale/consumption point. The tax on firewood is G
100/m3. These transportation taxes, to be paid into the "Special
Reforestation Fund" (Decree of November 20. 1972), should be used mainly for
nursery and reforestation activities. This fund is presently managed by SRF.
10.         The woodfuel tax is collected by agents of SRF, who deliver two
copies of the receipt to the truck driver; one of these two copies has to be
surrendered at the entry point to cities, of which there are about 11 posts
around Port-au-Prince. These copies have to be sent back to SRF for control
purposes. of the estimated 8 million bags of charcoal produced and consumed
yearly in urban areas, about 6 million bags are sold inside Port-au-Prince,
which should produce a revenue of G 6 million. For the latest year for which
statistics were available (1988/89), the income was G 600,000, or 10% of the
expected revenue.
11.         The discrepancy is due to insufficient supervision and corruption;
for instance: at the loading site, when a small cash payment is made in lieu
of the full tax; at the entry point, when a few bags are given in lieu of
surrendering the original transport receipt (which can then be used again);
and by other "arrangements" which are difficult to police.
12.         At the MARNDR headquarters level, where the books of transport
permits are delivered to the field forestry agents, the transactions &re
poorly handled, and there is no supervision at the top. The tax revenues and
permits delivered are not reconciled. The original receipts surrendered to
the entry points around Port-au-Prince (see para. 10 above) are not secured.
It is probable that these receipts are again sold to truck drivers for a
fraction ci their value. The implications of this situation are serious,
since the statistics produced by SRF about production of fuelwood, its
transformation into charcoal, and the movements of this c-',modity in the
country are based on the transport tax receipts.
13.         Very little can be done in the field as a result of the absence of
effective police, law and order and it is not until the existing laws (or new



-177-
amUa
Page 4 of 4
ones eventually promulgated) are implemented, that the proper collec-tion of
these taxes, and others au well, could be ensured. SRF has, at .aast, to keep
track of the distribution of these recelpt books (whlch are prLnted by SRF),
and the return of the used book, and reconcle- the revenues obtained with the
permlts and the receipts iesued. To improve the existLng situatlon and
prepare a strategy for the future, funds would be provlded under the project
for a short-term consultancy to revliw and pr pare recommendations for the
improvement of the woodfuel taxation system, the collectlon rates, the
adjustment of the tax levels to lntroduce economLc prLcLng of fuels, and
improved accountlng and budgetlng procedures for the Special ReforestatLon
Fund (see para. 9 above). The objectlve La to Lntroduce after the mld-term
revlew of the project a rational and efflclent taxatlon system, whlch would,
in addition to recover part of the socLal costs of deforestatlon, allow
flnanclng forestry development and envlronment protectlon actlvlties on a
sustained basis.

HAITI
PORESTRY AND ENVIRONHENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
Terms of Reference
For An International Firm of Management Consultants
1.          These terms of reference describe services to be contracted by the
Government of Haiti to an international firm of consultants for the provision
of management assistance and technical expertise that is required during the
implewentation of the Government's Forestry and Environmental Protection
Project. The project would be supported by a Credit from the International
Development Association (IDA).
2.          Obiectives of the Project.  The objective of the project would be
to slop down the pace of degradation of Haiti's natural resources and to
provide investment funds and institutional and policy mechanisms aimed ats
(a) protecting the forests and safeguarding vital ecosystems from further
destruction; (b) increasing wo.d production through the promotion of
agroforestry on farms and the improved management of State forest lands; and
(c) reducing the pressure on vegetation for charcoal production through
conservation of voodfuels used by urban households.
3.          Prolect Comyonents.  The project would provide technical and
financial support for: (a) the institutional strengthening of the Directorate
of Natural Resources (Direction des Ressources Naturelles - DRN) and to two of
its Services, the Forestry Resources Service (Service des Ressources
Foresti&res - SRP), and the Environmental Protection Service (Service de
Protection de l'Envirornement - SPE) and training in forestry and
agroforestry, all within the Ministry of Agriculture, Natural Resources and
Rural Development - ARNKDR; (b) specific programs to promote tree planting and
soil conservation, to impr -e the management of forest reserves, to expand
forestry and agroforestry research, to establish and manage national parks and
to monitor lh d use in Haiti; and (c) promotion of more efficient charcoal
stoves in urban areas by the Bureau of Mines and Energy (BME).
4.          Implementing Axencies.  Except fo- the Household Energy
Conservation Subproject (part (c) above, which would be implemented by BME
(Bureau des Mines et de l'Energie of the Ministry of Public Works, Transport
and Communications)), the project will be implemented by MARNDR through DRN.

would be implemented over five years, starting in October 1991. The

but extendable to five years pending the results of a mid-term review of
project implementation that would be carried out by independent consultants
during the third year of the project.
6.          LonE-Term Exoertisoe Requlred.  The following experts would be
raquired for the institutional strengthening programs within MARDR:
(a)   in DRN, one financial manager and one technical coordinator;
(b)   in SRF, a senior forester;
(c)   in SPE, a resident ecologist; and
(d)   in the Technical School for Agroforestry, a training specialist.
The first four positions will be for a period of three years, to
be extended to five at the time of the mid-term review of the project. The
training specialist is required for a period of two years.
7.          Short-Term Consultants Reauired.  In addition, there will be need
for short-term consultancies for about 12 assignments in the following fields:
(i) legal, for the review and update of the legal framework regulating
forestry and other natural resources; (11) In agroforestry and forestry
research; (iii) the study of woodfuel taxation; and (iv) determination and
support of necessary actions in the fields of remote sensing, survey
techniques, photo interpretation, ecology, sociology, and management of
biosphere reserves.

Terms of Reference
for the Mid-Term Review of the Prolect
1.           Obiectives  The mid-term review would assess the strengths and
weaknesses of the project at mid-point in light of the objectives specified at
appraisal, and draw lessons for project implementation during the remainder of
the financing period. It would be the basis for redirecting project
activities towards the most effective operations, if needed, and for the
design of specific actions geared to resolve major problems and shortfalls, if
any.
2.          Timing and Implementation Arrangements: The mid-term review would
be carried out by external consultants in close collaboration with supervisory
staff from the MARNDR and BME during the first half of the third year of the
project (October 1993 to March 1994). The consultants would be hired by
MARNDR and be independent from the project's management firm.
3.          Scope of the Reviews  The review would compare the achievements
during the start-up period (financed under the PPF) and first two project
years with the expectations and plane set out in the Staff Appraisal Report
and the Legal Documents. The review would include the following ma)or
aspects:
(a)     important changes made in project scope and design after project
effectiveness, and their impact on the original objectives and
agreementsl
(b)     adherence to the original project implementation schedules for
hiring of consultants, implementation of studies and their
recommended follow-up action, procurement of goods, implementation
of civil works, and regulatory or legislative decisions taken;
(c)     effectiveness of the programming, budgeting and reporting
arrangements (quality, comprehensiveness, timing, relevance);
(d)     progress in procurement of civil works, goods and hiring of
consultants;
(e)     comparison of actual expenditures with appraisal estimates, at the
project level, for each major component, and for each category;
(f)     comparison of actual disbursements from the credit with appraisal
expectations;



-186-
Appendix 4
Page 2 of 2
(8)     performance of consultants in terms of quality and quantity of
services provided and impact on project results: (i) project
management firm; (i) individual consultants; (iii) NGO
contractors (agroforestry extension and research, and household
energy); and (iv) external monitornSg agency;
(h)     assessment of the technical and managerial competence of the
Borrower and the overall control exercised on the project;
(1)     fulfillment of legal covenants of the Development Credit
Agreement;
Mj)     assessment of the achievement of institution-building objectives:
(i) quality of project management; (11) changes made to the
organizational structure; (iii) additional functions being carried
out under the project; and (iv) improvements or deterioration of
financial systems, budgetary controls and procedures, accounting,
Inventory controls, plant operation and maintenance, and vehicle
control;
(k)     staff recruitment and training: (i) increase in staff numbers
compared to appraisal estimates; (1) qualifications and
motivation of staff; (iii) salaries and allowances; (iv)
effectiveness of formal and in-service training; and (v) turn-over
of senior and middle management staff; and
(1)     contribution of the expatriates towards the efficient operation of
the project, towards establishing sound management and technical
systems and procedures, and towards developing local management
and technical staff.
4.          Based on the findings from these assessments, recommendations
should be prepared to redirect the project within the given objectives,
reallocate funds, and correct the deficiencies in project implementation.
These recommendations would be presented for review by the Government of Haiti
and the Bank, who will jointly decide on the further steps to be taken. A
partieular focus of the mid-term review would be the pilot program for the
construction of cisterns.

HAITI
FORESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
Ministry of Agriculture. Natural Resources and Rural DLXqlopne_=
Mninitere de l'Agriculturs. des Ressources Naturalles et du
Development Rural (MARNDR)
I. General Oroanization
1.          The authority of the Ministry of Agriculture, Natural Resources
and Rural DeveloPment (MARNDR) is based on the Organic Law of November, 1957
and is vested with the Minister of Agriculture, a member of the Council of
Ministers. Under him operates the Disector General of the Ministry who is in
charge of ranging and coordinates the three economic sectors of the Ministry.
that is: Agriculture, Natural Resources and Rurel Development. The General
Directorate itself is laterally strengthen'ed by a technical secretariat, a
Control and Evaluation Entity including the Ministry's Programming Unit and
the Center of Equipment.
2.          Down this line of authority, the technical and administrative
functions are broken down into five "Directorates." They include the
following entitiess
(a)   Directorate of Plan Production;
%b)   Directorate of Animal Productiont
(c)   Directorate of Natural Resources;
(d) Directorate of Rural Development; and
(e)   Directorate of Administration.
Also down the line of authority directly from the office of the "General
Directorate" are four Regional Directors who coordinate the 19 Agricultural
Districts in the country. Each district is intended to be a regional
representation of the Direction Generale and, therefore, mirrors the
Ministry's central structure.
3.          Inadequacles and weaknesses of the existing structure, among them
the limitations Ln qualifLed personnel and operational budget, have, howevo'r,
prevented the distrlcts from fully discharging their duties and
responsLbilties. The relative power of the central directorates causes that
certain functlons such as planning, personnel management, and even
implementation, are sometimes only nominally carried out with the
particLpation of the dlstrict manager. For instance, the services of the
Natural Resources Directorate have remalned particularly strong at the central
offlce level and acqulred a larger influence at the district level, while this



-188-
ANNEX a
Attachment A
Page 2 of 9
is not the case for the services of the Plant Production, Animal Production
and Rural Development directorates. One unfortunate development, for example,
is how the formerly omnipresent dominant agricultural extension service of the
Rural Development Directorate has lost scope and intensity in its activities.
4.          Another factor in the weakening of the agriculture districte is
the proliferation of specific projects financed by bilateral or multilateral
donors. Not willing to rely on the existing structures in the Ministry, some
projects established their own implementing agency, generally separateLy from
official channels, and with their own staff and expatriates enjoying level of
salaries and operating budgets not available to the official agencies of the
ministry of Agriculture. Some of these projects were designed to cover the
entire area of an agricultural district, taking over or duplicating, all the
functions of that district. These development projects, even those
express!vely designed to strengthen the districts, have in fact considerably
weakened the regional structures. In establishing temporarily a privileged
sector of activity, the equilibrium between the traditional activities is
upset and after a brief period of ictorial improvement the whole weakened
structure is left unassisted and non-operational after the completion of the
project, and the exhaustion of project funds
II. Structure and Functions of Each "Directorate"
5 .    (a)   Directorate of Plant Production.  Under the office of the Director
of Plant Production are two services for: (i) food and fruit
crops; and (ii) export and agroindustrial crops.
(b)   Directorate of Animal Production.  Under the office of the
Director of Animal Production are two services for: (i) animal
husbandry; and (ii) animal ht lth. However, plant protection and
animal protection are being handled jointly by a specialized
quarintine unit under the Director General.
(c)   Directorate of Administration.  Under the office of the Director
of Administration are seven services and administrative units for:
(i) budget; (ii) accounting; (iii) purchasing; (iv) supply;
(v) inventory; (vi) garage and transportation; and (vii) a center
for small and heavy equipment.
(d)   DLrectorate of Rural Develooment.  Under the office of the
Director of Rural Development are four services for:
(i) agricultural extension; (Li) rural crafts; (iii) cooperatives
and peasant groups; and (iv) rural youth.

6.          The Directorate of Natural Reaources (DRN), which would be the
main implementing agency of the project, is described in more detail:
7.          Of the three original technical divisions of the former Department
for Agriculture, Natural Resources and Rural Development (DARNDR), as the
present Ministry was called when created in 1957, Natural Resources has _-en
the most stable one. The only major change ham been the separation oi the
Mining Service which was set up first as an indeDendent office in the 1980g.
later became a Ministry of its own and was recently placed as Bureau of Mines
and Energy under the Minietry of Public Works Transport and Communications.
Therefore, the Natural Resources division (now a "Directorate") became since
exclusively concerned with renewable natural resources. The current structure
of DRN includees the following services:
(a)   Irrigation and Rural. Enoineerina Service.  Its main dutie-' are to
operate and maintain all public irrigation systems in the
Agricultural Districts. It participates in planning especially of
the small systems, while some of the larger systems are under the
jurisdiction of specialized semi-autonomous regional organization
of the MARNDR, such as ODVA and ODPG with which the service
cooperates. The irrigation and rural engineering service also
assists all entities of the Ministry in the design and planning of
civil engineering and structural work being realized by these
entities, especially in the rural areas.
(b)   The Water Resources Services.  It is a consolidation of the
Meteorological service and the former Hydrology Section of the
former Irrigation Service and primarily concerned with data
collection.
(c)   Watershed Management Service.  Its main duties are:  surveying and
inventory of soil conservation programs and assistance to peasants
in implementing conservation work at the farm level. Water
conservation, especially the construction of cisterns in the rural
area with water scarcity problems. has recently become an
additional focus. Planting of fruit and forest trees are also
being promoted. Seven professionals including those of the Soil
Survey Unit (3) make up the staff of this service at the
headquarters and a total of eight professionals and 40
subprofessionals of this service are spread in the various
districts and projects throughout the country.
(d)   Aauaculture Service.  The main attention of this service is
devoted to promote freshwater shrimps and other high-priced
freshwater species that are of interest to commercial ventures.
Present staff includes four agronomist and a few subprofessionals
that are, however, unable to provide adequate service for the
whole country.

ANNEX 8
Attachment A
Page 4 of 9
(e)   Marine Fisherieo Servige.  The service is seriously underfunded
and understaffed with only four professionals and six
subprofeseionals working as technicians and unable to carry out
the functions of regulation, statistical control and development
or promotion of marine fisheries.
(f)   Pores  Resourcea Service (SRF).  The service, supported by
previous IDA credit (Cr. 1257-HA) for a Pilot Forestry Project,
has evolved from a technical section of the former Service de
Conservation de Sole et des Forets, which used to deal mostly with
the administration of permits for cutting trees and other controls
to one of the best organized services of the Natural Resources
Directorate. In view of its special importance in project
implementation a complete treatment of this Service is given in
Section III below of this Attachment A. It describes its present
situation, and includes a description of its oxpected role under
the project.
(g)   Environmental Protection Service (SPE).  The main duties of this
relatively new service ares (i) take the necessary steps to
protect endangered plant and animal species as well as valuable
ecosystems; (ii) realize and update baseline studies on natural
resources in order to develop their production potential in
conjunction with environment protection; (iii) recommend areas
where parks and other reserved entities to be protected should be
established; and (iv) recommend, perform or participate in the
realization of environment impact studies of development projects
and regional planning studies of the country. The service is
supposed to be organized in three technical sections: (i) research
and inventory; (ii) preservation of species and natural sites; and
(iii) pollution prevention and protection. The technical
personnel of the service is limited to three agronomists, one of
them being the chief of service and one of them assigned to the La
Visits National Park. Most of the wardens in the existing
national two parks (La Visite and Macaya) are actually being, like
in the past, supervised by the SFR, since this new service has not
yet been given adequate means to operate in the field, nor to
undertake this assigned duties.

III. Oroanization of the Forestrv Regource Service
for Proiect Imolementation
Present Situation
8.          The Forest Resources Service (SRF) is nominally divided into four
sectionss (a) Natural Forest Management; (b) Reforestation and Agroforestry;
(c) Forest Statistics; and (d) Forest Research. An administrative support
staff of approximately 19 members work under the supervision of the SRF Chief.
Each section is supervised by a Section Head who reports directly to the SRF
Chief. With PFN support, the Natural Forest Management Section is primarily
engaged in the management of the Pine Forest Reserve. Three agronomists and
17 technicians are assigned to the Pine Forest, four technicians are assigned
to mangrove areas and one technician to the pine forest areas of northeastern
Haiti. The Reforestation and Agroforestry Section, which la primarily
responsible for the reforestation of four parcels of SRF managed State land,
employs (near the villages of: Cabaret, Colora, Colladare and Jean Rabel) six
agronomists and 14 technicians. The Forest Statistics Section is responsible
for che rontrol of timber harverting and wood product transport, and the
collection of the associated taxes. This section employs three technicians,
30 wood product controllers, and coordinates the work of 60 forestry agents.
These forestry agents work under the direct supervision of the 19 agricultural
districts.
9.          The Protet Forestler National (PFN) primarily strengthened the
Pine Forest Reserve management activities of the Natural Forest Management
Section, and the reforestation research activities of the Forest Research
Section. Program support is thus lacking for the management of natural
forests other than the Pine Forest, and for other types of forestry research
(e.g., agroforestry and semi-arid forest management). The Reforestation and
Agroforestry Section program activity is limited to the PFN supported
reforestation of the above-mentioned four State land parcels. The Forest
Statistics Section was not directly targeted for PFN assistance, the employees
under its supervision receive no significant program support or training, and
the administrative systems remain inadequate. Though programmatically one of
the most active Services in the DRN, the SRF does not provide an effective and
comprehensive response to the extensive and multiple forestry problems of
Haiti.
Obiectives and Strateav
10.         The obiectives of the SRF institutional support program are to:
(a)   establish SRF's overall capacity to supervise, monitor, finance
and evaluate forestry and agroforestry activities, including those
implemented by NGOs, thus building a foundation for larger
forestry development investments in the future;



-192-
ANNEX 8
Attachment A
Page 6 of 9
(b)   increase the wood and agricultural productivity and standard of
living of peoples within and surrounding important forest reserves
to stabilize natural resource utilization;
(c)   enhance the management the Pine Forest Reserve for sustained wood
production and the protection of biodiversity;
(d)   enhance forestry and agroforestry research and initiate pilot
forest management programs on State owned semi-arid forest and
savanna lands to learn of what social and technical interventions
are required to make these lands more productive;
(e)   create new forestry reserves on State lands with a forestry
vocation, with a goal to improve the productivity of the lands and
increase the supply of wood products to urban markets;
(f)   enhance the administrative capabilities of the SRF including the
fiscalization of the wood product taxes and land rents from the
SRF managed Reserve lands; and
(g)   develop the Reforestati3n and Agroforestry Section as an
Institution which provides technical assistance and personnel
support to agroforestry extension efforts lad by the SRF and
implemented exther directly or indirectly through NGOs.
11.         General Strateav.   The Forestry and Environmental Protection
Project (FEPP) would izitervene at the head and section level of the SRF. The
SRF Chief would be supported with long-term technical and administrative
assistance. Each of the SRF's four sectione would be responsible for the
direct implementation of certain project activities.   These sections would be
supported with short-term technical assistants and substantial training
opportunities. The provieion of agroforestry services to farmers living in
and near protected ecosystems would be contracted to qualified NGOs whose work
would be supervised and evaluated by the Reforestation and Agroforestry
Section.
Proiect Components
A. Institutional Development of the Forest Resources Service
12.         Internal Evaluation and Planning.  The SRF would conduct annual
internal evaluation and planning sessions in which all of the professional
staff would participate. This process would take place prior to the end of
each fiscal year and result in detailed Section and Service program plans for
the proximate fiscal year. The process would be initiated at the Section
level where all Section employees would participate. Upon completion of that
initial round, the senior staff (including Section Heads, service Chief and
technical assistants) would convene to evaluate and program the activities of



-193-
ANNX 8
Attachment A
Page 7 of 9
the entire Service. The Section evaluations and plans would serve as the
point of departure for this Service session. Each session would be conducted
in a participatory fashion and led by an independent, professional group
facilitator. The groups would initially evaluate past performance,
identifying program strengths and weakneeses. Special effort would be devoted
to understanding the specific causes of both the program weaknesses and
exceptional strengtho. The group would then set the goals and objectives for
the proximate year, and follow with descriptions of separate programs. After
completion, the Section Heads would revise their Section plans accordingly,
and the SRF Chief would write the SRF evaluation and plan. A program
monitoring system would be followed-up to register progress.
13.         Training.  Training events would be conducted for all personnel
within the SRF. This training would include periodic events such as annual
technical retreats for each personnel rank and in-service training;
occasional training such as special short courses, graduate scholarships for
experienced agronomists, and the formal preparation of new agroforestry
technicians. The training component is deecribed in detail in Annex 7.
14.         Administrative Systems.   Improved administrative systems would be
initiated. These would include the fiscalization of the tax funds collected
through the Forest Statistics Section (the Special Reforestation Fund), and
the land rents collected in the forest reserve areas. New computer systems
would be installed to assist in this effort. Improved tax collection systems
would also be initiated in the effort to improve SRF revenues (see para. 9 of
Annex 8).
B. Forest Statistics Section
15.         The responsibilities of this Section would be expanded from the
control of wood products and the collection of taxes, to include the
coordination of all forestry protective functions. Thus, in addition to
supervising the forestry agents and wood product controllers, the Head of this
Section would coordinate the work of the forest rangers and the military
forest guards. The purpose of this change is to centralize the control and
protective functions and facilitate the imple,enntation of coherent policies
and programs. The Pine Forest Reserve ranger supervisor then would report to
the Head, and it is the Head of this Section who would liaise with the
appropriate army officer responsible for the deployment of the forest guards.
16.         Personnel and folest
(a)   Technician.  The section would employ one technician in the
central office to assist the Section Head in the collection of
data and the coordination of the agent and controller ranks;



-194-
ANNEX 
Attachment A
Page 8 of 9
(b) Forestry Agents. These 60 personnel would continue to be directly
supervised by the separate agricultural districts. Their primary
role would continue to consist of regulating the harvest of timber
and issuing transport papers. They would receive training on
regulation an well as other fundamental topics (as described in
Annex 7).
(c) Wood Product Controllers. The duties of these 30 personnel, who
largely are responsible for the collection of wood product taxes.
would not change under the project. These personnel would receive
training to improve their performance (as described in Annex 7).
(d) Forest Rangers. These personnel would be charged with the
protection of the forest reserves, the majority of who would work
in the Pine Forest Reserve under the ranger supervisor. New
rangers would be hired and trained as new forest reserves are
identified and established. These personnel would receive
training (as described in Annex 7).
(e) Ranger Supervisor. This individual would be assigned to the Pine
Forest Reserve and would be supervised in the field by the Pine
Forest Reserve forester. He would supervise the rangers and
ensure active cooperation with the locally stationed military
forestry guards.
(f) Military Forestry Guards. These personnel, who are assigned to
the Pine Forest Reserve. would report directly to the ranger
supervisor. They would man the official checkpoints located at
various sites in the Pine Forest Reserve.
C. Reforestation and Aaroforestrv Section
17.         Aaroforestrv SuvDort.  The project would assist the Reforestation
and Agroforestry Section in becoming an effective leader and promoter of
agroforestry extension in Haiti. This role could eventually entail direct
technical assistance through the agricultural districts, the contracting of
agroforestry extension to qualified NG0s. and possibly the creation of a
nation-wide agroforestry extension service directly implemented by the
Section. In order to achieve the above goal, Section foresters (six) require
additional technical agroforestry, and project management experience. As the
SRF does not directly implement agroforestry projects (and direct
implementation is not proposed in the project), the six agronomists assigned
to this Section would be seconded to other institutions currently implementing
agroforestry projects. These could include the NGOs which receive contracts
for the extension of agroforestry services (see below).



-195-
Attachment A
Page 9 of 9
18.         Aoroforeatrv Extension in National Parks and Reserves.
Agroforestry extension projects would be implemented by contractod NGO0 within
and adjacent to the Pic Macaya and La Visite national parks, the Pine Forest
Reserve, and in the upper elevations of the Belle Anse area. The purpose of
these projects would be to develop a partnership between local peasants and
the responsible Government agency which increases the overall productivity and
ensures protection of the special zones. These projects are described in
detail in Annex 5.
D. Aatural Forest Manaaement Section
19.         Management of the Pine Forest Reserve.  The project would continue
and strengthen the Pine Forest management activities conducted during the PFN.
The purpose of this component is to develop the Pine Forest Reserve as a
substantial producer of timber and wood products in Haiti. These activities
are described in detail in Annex 3.
20.         Creation of New Forest Reserves.  The Natural Forest Management
Section is nominally responsible for the proper management of all natural
forests on State lands assigned to Ministry of Agriculture. To date, the Pine
Forest Reserve is the only natural forest which is managed by the SRF. The
SPE would identify, inventory and delineate new forestry reserves. The lawyer
within the DRN would assure the legal transfer of title to the Ministry of
Agriculture. These reserves would form a public, forestry domain which would
be managed by this Section for the production of wood products. This
management would result in both the protection of natural ecosystems but also
the export of wood products to urban centers.
E. Forestry Research Section:
21.         Towards the production of critical technical and social
information which would permit large-scale forestry development in the future;
the project would support substantial agroforestry, pine forest management,
semi-arid forest management, and savanna reforestation research. These
activities are described in Annex 4.
