HAITI
FORESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
Household Enerav Conservat4on Subtoroject
I. Intr duction ard Backggound
1. *This description of the Household Energy Conservation Subproject
is divided into four sections: (a) an introduction which provldes background
on the relationship between forest products and charcoal use, the structure of
demand for charcoal, Haitian cooking habits and cooking equipment, and
existing institutions and efforts seeking to conserve cooking energy; (b) the
rationale for undertaking a cooking efficiency program within the context of
the Forestry and Environmental Protection Project (FEPP), along with
objectivee for the subproject; (c) a scope of work which describes the
ccmponents of the subproject: new product development, artisanal training.
public relations/education campaign, and monitoring; and (d) a management
section which outlines institutional arrangements, the statue of the sub-
project within the FEPP, an implementation schedule and a budget. In
addition, annexes provide projected results, financial and economic cost-
benefit analyses, and technical data on cookstovesY
2.          Forest Products and Enerav Demand.  The most important source of
energy in Haiti is woodfuel (firewood and charcoal) which, in 1989. accounted
for over three-quarters of the 1.4 million tons of oil equivalent (TOE)
conaumed rationally by all sectors of the economy.  In 1990, it is estimated
that consumers will use 300.000 tons of charcoal and 2.3 million tons of wood.
In wood-equivalent terms, this amounts to 3.8 million tons of air-dry wood, or
5.4 million m3. The majority of firewood consists of dead wood, pollarded
branches and brushwood collected by rural dwellers, while charcoal is
primarily produced from clear-cut green wood and small-diameter fallow shrubs.
This demand for wood energy, especially for charcoal, is one of the driving
force behind the unsustainable consumption of forest resources in Haiti, along
with the fact that charcoal production is an important source of rural income.
If current demand trends continue unabated, analyses indica'e that national
woody biomass resources could be theoretically depleted in the next fifteen
years.
Unless otherwise noted, data used in this paper come from previous
FEPP reports prepared by Messrs. Leitmann (November 1989) and
Feinstein (April 1990). and the draft World Bank/UNDP ESMAP Energy
strategy for Households and Small Enterprises (June 1990).



-202-
ANNEX 2
Page 2 of i
3.          Thg Structure of Char6oal Demand.  Becauoe the supply and demand
of charcoal has beea isolated as a major cause of deforestation, this analysis
will focus on carbonized wood, as opposed to firewood consumption. Two-thirds
of charcoal demand iu; accounted for by households, with the remainder being
consumed by the informal sector, primarily by sellers of Izanje kwlt"
(roadside fast food). Spatially, charcoal use lo an urban phenomenon: in
1990, it is estimated that 200,000 tons will be used in Port-au-Prince
(160,000 tons by households and 40,000 tons by the informal sector), and
another 100,000 tons will be consumed in secondary cities (80,000 tons
households. 20,000 tons informal sector). Thus, less than one quarter of the
Haitian population consumes 1.5 million tons of wood-equivalent annuallv, or
40% of all wood energy used throughout the country.
4.          gooking Habits and REuinment.  In Port-au-Prince, charcoal is used
as a cooking fuel by 92% of all households, with 62% using it as their sole
source of cooking energy and 20% of all residences consuming charcoal as their
principal cooking fuel. Charcoal ia the dominant cooking fuel for all socio-
economic groups except for the wealthiest quintile, where only 41% of
households use it as their exclusive or principal sourca o} cooking energy.
Averaged over the population of the capital, estimated daily charcoal
consumption ranges between 0.36 and 0.42 kg per person. In addition to
households, there are approximately 20,000 sellers of "manje kwit" i.l .ort-au-
Prince, or 1 per 8 households, and a similar ratio in secondary cities. This
informal sector activity accounts for about 20% of urban charcoal demand.
Households use two types of charcoal cooktovos: (a) the 'recho rond," a
circular metal stove (diameter: 20 cm) jriced at around G 2; and (b) the
"recho potaje," a two- or three-burner elevated metal stove costing G 15-20.
Informal sector food vendors generally use a larger version of the recho rond
(diameter: 30 cm) which sells for G 3-5. Most of these stoves are made from
recycled metal by informal sector artisans. The recho rond is produced by an
estimated 200 stovemakers in the capital (100 in the secondary cities) while
the recho potaje is made by ironworkers.
5.          Inotitutions and Proarams.  The Direction des Ressources
Energetiques of the Bureau des Mines et de l'Energie (BME) in the Ministere
des Travaux Publics, Transports et Communications has been primarily
responsible for cooking efficiency programs during the 1980s, in cooperation
with international donors and NGOs. Past and present efforts have consisted
of:
(a)   an improved cookstove component financed by the Canadian
ICRD under the IDA-supported Projet Forestier National.
Between 1983 and 1986, this US$90,000 activity resulted in
training. establiohment of a 3tove-testing laboratory,
surveys, and production of an improved recho rond prototype
(hereafter called the BME model);
(b)   an ongoing ILO-supported program to train artisans in Jacmel
and Grisgris to produce BME stoves;



-203-
Page 3 of 9
(c)   starting in 1989, an AFVP (French volunteers organization)
effort which hoo trained ten stovemakers to make elevated
and standard versions of the BME stove made of recvcled
metal for food vendorsl
(d)   a US$15,000 BME effort, begun in 1989 with support from
OLADE, for the pilot dissemination of the improved recho
rond. Three workshops have employed six artisane to produce
400 stoves made of new metal in the capital. Between
February and June, 1990, 50 have been Bold to different
households whose reactions are being monitored; and
(e)   a small Swiss NGO, REDI, has been active in 1989/90 installing an
efficient wood and charcoal-burning stove in several restaurants
and institutional kitchens.
Over seven years, these efforts have cost about Us$120,000 and resulted in the
dissemination of only 600 improved stoves. Some lessons learned include:
(a) the BME model is acceptable to consumers and yields theoretical charcoal
savings of 30% over the traditional recho rond (20-25% in practice); (b) the
AFVP approach of using recycled metal and individual artisans results in a
more affordable product than the BME system relying on new metal and workshops
(G 4 per stove versus G 7); and (c) a mass dissemination effort, with large-
scale training and a professional public relations campaign, is needed if
improved stoves are to achieve significant market penetraAion.
''. Rationale and Obiectiveo
6.          Energy-driven deforestation in Haiti can be addressed in three
ways: by increasing the supply of woody biomass with reforestation and better
management of the natural forest cover, through accelerated switching from
woodfuel to petroleum products, and from energy conservation. The forestry
and environmental components of the project will address the supply and
management option. Distortions in the substitute fuel market (monopoly rents,
inequitable taxation of fuels and equipment) cannot be resolved within the
context of a forestry project. Market tests suggest that, even with fuel
switching to LPG and kerosene, 60% of urban households will continue to cook
with charcoal. Thus, if the FEPP is to take a comprehensive approach to
protecting national forest resources, it must address both the supply and
demand sides. The most promising means of pronoting energy conservation
amongst urbanites, i.e., reducing the most environmentally-damaging form of
woodf3el demand, is by a cooking efficiency program aimed at lowering
household and informal sector consumption of charcoal. This will entail
large-scale private sector production of improved cookstoves, couipled with
changes in cooking habits.
7.          The objective of this subproject is to achieve a 10% reductlon in
charcoal demand at the end of the project (1997) by encouraging improved stove



-204-
ANEX9
Page 4 of 9
uae by half of the urban households nationwide that will continue to cook with
charcoal. In designing the subproject, the following guiding principles are
retained:
(a)   the deaLgn and promotLon of stoves ia targeted to satisfy useros
needs (convenience, affordabllity, durability, etc.);
(b)   maximum use La made of the private sector for stove
production and marketing, and NGOs for training and
monitoring;
(c)   diversity is encouraged in both the production process and
types of products offered to consumers; and
(d)   aside from needed training and marketing support, no subsLdy
is made to producers or consumers of improved stoves.
8.          The expected outputs of the subproject are as follows:
(a)   a total of 2109000 improved stoves disseminated over the
period 1991-1996. with a peak dissemination rate of 68,000
stoves per year reached in 1996;
(b)   cumulative savings of 41.000 tons of charcoal between 1991
and 1996, reaching 10% of total urban household charcoal
consumption in 1996;
(c)   a stove production network of over 150 trained artisans
marketing their output independently and through established
stove retailers;
(d)   a public education and promotion campaign to encourage
consumers to use improved stoves and adopt energy-conserving
behavior; and
(e)   a monitoring program to maintain stove quality and evaluate
actual charcoal savings and other benefLts.
These outputs are conservatively based on the assumption that distortions in
the substitute fuel market will be reduced and fuel switching will accelerate.
If this does not occur, then the subproject should reach a larger target
market.
III. Scope of Work
9.          The Household Energy Conservation Subproject consiets of four
coordinated components: (a) new product development; (b) artieanal training;



-205-
P.NNEX 9
Page 5 of 9
(c) a public relations and education campaign; and (d) monitoring and
evaluation of results. A brief description of each component is presented
below. An implementation schedule and budget covering all four components are
presented in the following section.
10.         New Product DeveloDment.  As previously mentioned, an acceptable
improved recho rond has been developed by the BME and is undergoing pilot
dissemination. Market studies indicat, that 60% of households in Port-au-
Prince are currently using the traditional recho rond. However, another 22%
of families use the traditional recho potaje; to date, no improved potaje has
been developed. Therefore, if the full market of charcoal consumers is to be
reached through a conservation program, an improved model of the recho potaje
should be developed which can be manufactured through the existing production
system, conserves energy, is cost effective and is acceptable to the consumer.
11.         To develop this new product, the following steps would be taken:
(a)   efficiency testing (boiling and specific consumption) of the
recho potaje, and evaluation of the artisanal production
system;
(b)   development of a prototype improved potaje, working with one
or more artisans to design a model which can be produced by
existing ironworkers with local materials at an affordable
price;
(c)   production of 50 improved potajes for sale to households
and/or food vendors and monitoring of these users to
evaluate whether the stove is acceptable to consumers and to
estimate actual efficiency gains from its use; and
(d)   re-design of the prototype, if necessary, based on results
of the consumer acceptability testing, to produce a
marketable model.
This work will be carried out by existing staff at the BME, with the short-
term technical assistance of an internationally recruited Cookstove Expert.
Some upgrading of the BME stove testing laboratory will be required.
12.         Artisanal Traininq.  Four groups of artisans will undergo training
in the construction and marketing of improved stoves: (i) stovemakers in
Port-au-Prince (estimated totelt 200) who produce the recho rond; (ii)
ironworkers in Port-au-Prince who make the recho potaje (70); (iii)
stovemakers in the secondary cities (100); and (iv) ironworkers in the
secondary cities (35). For the purposes of this subproject, secondary cities
include Cap-Haitien, Gonaives, Les Cayes and Port-de-Paix.
13.         Training will consist of a one-week course covering basic
thermodynamics, the economic and environmental rationale for household energy
conservation, design of the improved stove model, production techniques, an
outline of the public relations/education campaign, marketing methods for



-206-
ANNEX9
Page 6 of 9
artieans to lncrease saleo to retailers and consumers, and feedback from the
participants. During the course, artisans will receive a per diem to
compensate them for lost productlon time. Participants who successfully
complete the course will receive a tool kit, templates for cutting the metal
pieces of the improved atove, fifty quallty control stickers (see "Monitoring
and Evaluation" below), and a graduation certificate.
14.         A local NGO would be contracted to conduct the training sessions
and recruLt participants. The Cooketove Expert would assist with the design
and implementation of the first sessions. The first course would be designed
for 25 stovemakere from Port-au-Prince, plus five trainers (two from Cap-
Haitien and one each from the other cities), and would focus on production of
the 20 and 30 cm improved recho rond. Artisans would have several incentives
to participate in the training: free technical assistance, receipt of a tool
kit and templates, free advertising for a new product, and inclusion in the
quality control system. It is anticipated that half of the stovemakers and
ironworkers in the capital and secondary cities will eventually go through the
training course. All training would stress the use of recycled materials
because of their availability and lower cost.
15.         Publicity and Education CamDaians.  A local public relations firm
would be contracted to undertake programs to educate the public about the
linkage between charcoal consumption and environmental degradation, and to
market the improved recho models to households and food vendors. The
objectives of these campaigns would be tos (a) heighten environmental
awareness; (b) stress the practical advantages of the stoves (charcoal
savings, faster cooking time, greater control over heat output, longer
lifetime than traditional model); (c) emphasize, in simple terms, the concept
of a "payback period where the marginally higher cost of improved stoves is
quickly made up in reduced charcoal consumption. e.g., by visually depicting
that each  mproved stove saves the user three sacks of charcoal per year (see
Appendix 2 Economic and Financial Returns); and (d) promote other means of
reducing hcusehold consumption of charcoal, e.g., soaking beans overnight
prior to c(oking them, using a lid with cooking pots, use of aluminum pots and
pans, etc.
16.         These campaigne have been instrumental in stimulating improved
cookstove emand in a number of recent successful projects, notably those in
Kenya (the Jiko stoves), Niger (the Mai Sauki in Niger) and Rwanda (Kigali).
Some of tht promotional techniques which merit inclusion in the Haitian effort
include:
neighborhood demonstrations, with skits, music and cooking
competitions, to demonstrate the advantages of the improved
product;
billboards which inform consumers about the existence and benefits
of the new products;



-207-
ANN4EX 9
Page 7 of 9
-     a contest among music groups to produce an improved stove song.
and a competition in schools to develop a name or logo for the
improved recho rond and potaje;
-     TV and radio spots which make use of the song and logo;
-     t-shirts and decals; and
-     a van with loudspeakers to visit neighborhoods, provide quick
demonstrations of the new products and inform consumers where they
can be purchased.
These techniques will be geared to selected consumer target groups.
implemented in a multi-phase campaign with an interim evaluation and re-
design.
17.         Xonjiorina and Evaluation.  A NGO will be contracted to undertake
a variety of follow-up activities with the primary objective of ensuring that
the subproject's goal of increasing household cooking efficiency is attained.
Specific monitoring and evaluation tasks include:
(a)   an initial consumer survey, conducted four months after the
beginning of stove sales and the promotion campaign to
assess whethert (i) the product is responding to user
needs; (ii) the improved stove is being used properly; and
(iii) other efficiency improvement measures included in the
public education campaign are being adopted. This will
entail interviewing 100-150 households which have purchased
the new product. This sample will be drawn from a list of
purchasers which the NGO will be responsible for maintaining
through regular visits to artisans;
(b)   undertaking quality control spot-checks by periodically
visiting all producers of the new stoves, randomly selecting
several stoves from their stock and using a checklist to
assess whether a bonafide improved model is being produced.
If the arti an passes this inspection, (s)he will receive up
to fifty n   -uality control stickers to attach to future
stoves.  At. _nspector from the NGO will visit the artisans
who received training every two months to conduct the
quality control and, at the same time, collect data on stove
sales and purchasers. "Copy-cat" artisans who did not go
through training but who are producing imitations of the
improved product can alsc itquest inspections and, if their
stoves pass, will be eligible to receive the stickers.
Stove producers who sell more t' n fifty stoves every two
months can visit the NGO or request a supplemental
inspection in order to receive more labels;



-208-
ANNE-X 9
Page 8 of 9
(c) through its regular visits to artisans, the NGO will keep
data on stove production, sales and selling prices over
time. This information will be compiled in a quarterly
report which will be made available to the BME. the co-
financing agency, the NOo responsible for training, and the
public relations firm; and
(d) at the mid-term (end-1992) and at the conclusion of the sub-
project (mid-1994), the NOO will prepare an evaluation
report which will estimate total charcoal savings (in
physical and monetary terms) due to use of the improved
product, as well as other benefits, i.e., reduced
expenditure on cooking equipment due to the stove'w longer
lifetime. These reports would be based on a two-week
program of weighing cooking fuels and interviewing stove
users. A representative sample would be drawn from the
lists of purchasers in the capital and the secondary cities
such that results will be statistically valid at the 95%
confidence level. These reports will also provide data on
the percentage of improved stove users who switch, either
back to traditional cooking equipment or to petroleum
products, and the reasons why they did so.
IV. MANAGEMENT OF THE SUBPROJECT
18.         This section covers a number of managerial issuess
Ci) identification and listing of the responsibilities of the key subproject
counterpart; (ii) institutional support to the counterpart; (iii) an
implementation schedule; and (iv) a budget.
19.         The Countornart.  The BME's Direction des Ressources Energetiques
will implement the subproject with support from ESMAP, the UNDP/World
Bank/bilateral donor supported Energy Sector Management Assistance Program, or
a management firm. The BME will name a staff member whose qualifications are
acceptable to IDA to serve fulltime as Conservation Coordinator. The
Coordinator, along with other 8HE and Government staff as necessary, will be
responsible for:
(a)   assessing the capabilities and interest of relevant NGOs and
public relations firms to define a short list that will be
asked to bid for the training, publicity and monitoring
contracts;
(b) preparing and conducting the call for bids, evaluating the
bids and selecting competent subcontractors;
(c) executing specific contracts with two NGOO and one public
relations firm, including the detailed nature and timetable
of activities, and expected outputs;



-209-
ANNEX 9
Page 9 of 9
(d)   reorienting the program and renegotiating existing
contracts, as necessitated in yearly reviews; and
(e)   carrying out mid-term and terminal evaluations of the sub-
project. using, Inte_a.lia, results of the monitoring sub-
contractor's raports.
20.         BME staff will also be involved in the new product development,
particularly in potaje testing. For this, they will work closely with the
Cookstove Expert who will undertake a short-term consultancy of three months.
Other existing BME staff who will be involved in the program are: the Director
of Energy Resources (10% of his time); and a survey specialist (33% time) and
statistician (33% time) to work with the monitoring NGO. Newly recruited
staff would be: an administrative assistant to help with report preparation
and supervision of subcontractors, an energy economist (50% time; see below)
who would monitor stove sales and market prices, as well as charcoal sales
(based on a weighing program) and price structures, and a driver.
21.         Institutional Sunoort.  To develop its institutional capabilities,
the BME will receive several types of institutional support: training,
equipment, and hiring of fixed-term personnel. Training will consist of an
initial two-week study tour for the Conservation Coordinator and the Director
of Energy Resources to visit successful cooketove dissemination programs
(possibly Mali, Rwanda, Kenya, and/or efforts in the LAC region). Equipment
will consist of material to upgrade the stove testing laboratory, a 4x4
vehicle (with funds for operating and maintenance expenses) and receipt of the
stove promotion van at the end of the publicity campaign. Hiring fixed-term
personnel will involve covering the costs of employing the energy economist,
administrative assistant and driver for the three-year life of the sub-
project. In addition to conservation-related responsibilities, the energy
economist would periodically assess the economic value of wood and charcoal.
This information would then be provided to the MARNDR to assist them in
evaluating charcoal taxes.
22.         Implementation Schedule.  A preliminary calendar for implementing
the subproject over a three-year period is outlined in Figure 1 below,
including inputs from the various actors involved. This calendar will be
modified by BME, in consultation with the Bank, as merited b- new information
and developments such as results from the OLADE-financed pilot diffusion
effort.
23.         Budget.  A budget, broken down by fiscal year, is presented below
in Table 1. The subproject has an estimated economic rate of return of 57%,
and the improved recho rond has a simple payback time of 55 days, based on
monthly savings of 9.8 kg of charcoal per month. The projected impact of
accelerated cookstove dissemination is presented in Appendix 1 to this Annex,
including net charcoal savinge. Financial and economic analyses, based on
these savings, are presented in Appendix 2. Appendix 3 contains data on
existing and improved cooketove costs and efficiencies. Appendix 4 contains
data on the economic and financial prices of charcoal.

FORESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROTECTION PROJECT
The Economic Price Range of Charcoal
This note briefly presents the results of three methodologies which
can be used to calculate the economic price range for charcoal in Haiti. The
three methods ares (a) border pricing of the nearest substitute fuel (page 3
of Appendix 4); (b) replacement value pricing (page 4 of Appendix 4); and
(c) marginal environmental cost pricing (pages 5 and 6 of Appendix 4). The
most conservative approach (border pricing using kerosene as the substitute
fuel) yields an economic price for charcoal of US$13S per metric ton. By
taking the replacement cost of wood based on industrial fuelvood plantations
in Haiti, the economic cost of charcoal is US$176 per ton. The most broad-
reaching approach, incorporating marginal tmvironmental costs, results in an
economic valuation of US$295 per ton. Thus, the three methodologies result in
an economic price range between US$139.2295 per ton, or US$5.30, 6.70 and 11.20
per 38 kg sack.  Converting this to an -..-!tomic price in Haitian gourdes/sack,
the respective value are 37, 47 and 78 Wii-rdzas. In comparison with the
existing financial price of 41.9 gourdaslsack, the economic price range is 88-
1862 of the market price.
Thus, taker at face value, the e..mic price range gives t.o
guidance as to the direction that fiscal pci ucy should take in guiding retail
charcoal prices.  Subjective decisions must UA made as to which economic
pricing methodology is moot appropriate to us. in the case of Haiti.  Each has
its drawbacks and advantages. Border pricini: Lt-sed on the least-cost
substitute household fuel (kerosene) is unre&lictic ast  (a) market tests
indicate that kerosene is not a preferred fuel for urban Haitian consumers;
(b) it is unlikely that a significant number of charcoal users would switch to
kerosene; and (c) it is doubtful that the neceo'sary foreign exchange could be
made available to pay for massive substitution with an imported fuel.
Replacement costing also has its disadvantagess  (a) no industrial fuelvood
plantations exist in Haiti so the estimated replacement cost of wood is
subject to uncertainty; and (b) attempting to replace all wood used for
charcoal production from plantations would run up against a number of
constraints, e.g.* availability of land and other key inputs.  The central
problem with marginal envirormental costing is the paucity of accurate data on
key variables such as the rate of soil erosion, the fertilizer value of tree
species and the degree of siltation caused by tree removal in representative
deforested areas of the country.
GIren the lim4tations of each method, but mindful of the need to
arrive at a recommendation regarding economic pricing, a choice should be made
as to which approach is moet appropriate.  The kerosene parity price can be
dropped as it is not a real-world option.  Replacement prlcing is also



-218-
ANNEX 9
Appendix 4
Page 2 of 7
unrealistic as industrial planting is unlikely to occur on a massive scale.
However, smallholder tree planting is occurring on a large scale and may be a
more reasonable proxy for replacement costs. Based on smallholder
reforestation costs, the replacement value of charcoal is US$300/ton (see
page 7 of Appendix 4). Therefore, an appropriate economic value for charcoal
in Haiti appears to be in the range of US$295-3001ton, i.e., between the
environmental and smallholder replacement costs. Thie implies a target retail
price of 78-80 gourdes/sack, or an increase of almost 90S above the present
price level.

Page 3 of 7
HAITI
FORESTRY AND ENVIRONMENTAL PROJECTION PROJECT
PaXriy Price of Least-Cost Substitute Fuel
The parity price of the least-cost substitute fuel is the economic value of the amount
of kerosene required to deliver the same amount of energy as charcoal, taking into account the life-
cycle costs of cooking equipment. This value is given by the equation:
PP = RV. - RVC * (LC. - LC)/ECC
where
PP    =     Parity Price
RV0   =     Replacement Value of charcoal
=     $176.40/ton (see Appendix 2)
LC5   =     Life-qcle Cost of charcoal (improved stove)
=     $23.02 per delivered GJ
LC,  =      Life-cycle Cost of kerosene (pressure stove)
=      i$18.25 per delivered GJ
EC.  =      Energy Cost of charcoal (improved stove)
=     $22.53 per delivered GJ
Plugging these values into the equation, one arrives at an economic substitute or parity price of
$139/ton of charcoal.

Marginal Environmental Value of Charcoal
From a recent analysis of the economic value of wood in Haiti (Hosier and
Bernstein, 1989), the marginal opportunity cost of a resource should rerxt its long-run marginal
value based on both its immediate environmental effects and the needs of future generations.
Mathematically, this can be expressed as:
MOC, = MC + MECi
where MOC, is the marginal opportunity cost of the resource, MC is the direct harvesting costs or
marginal costs as normally perceived and MEC, is the marginal external cost of the good to
different sectors.
In this analysis, the harvesting cost (MC) is considered to be equal to the financial
price of the woodfuel to the consuming sector. This is currently, at most, 35 - 50 Gourdes per
tonne (US$5-7/tonne). The marginal external contribution of trees to soil improvement (MEq)
is between US$12-20/TOE or US$4-6/tonne. The on-site contribution of trees to the reduction of
soil erosion (MECQ) is US$45/TOE or US$14/tonne. The off-site contribution of trees to sediment
reduction is calculated at US$10/TOE or US$3/tonne.
Therefore, the range for the economic value of wood in Haiti can be summed up as
follows:
Element                        Economic Value
(US$/tonne)
MC                                          5-7
MEC, (soil improvement)                     4-6
MEC2 (soil erosion)                          14
MEG3 (sedimentation)                          3
MOC,                                      26-30
Thus, if no lower-cost environmental damage avoidance measures are available, the economic
opportunity of wood on the stump in Haiti can be taken as between US$26-30 per tonne (US$18-
21 per m3).

BASTZ
FORESTXRY MD ENVIRONNENTfL PROTECTION PROJECT
LETTER ON SECTOA POLICY 
Rena Pr6val, Prime Minister
Ministry of the Interior and National Defense, National Palace
No. CPM/0568                                    Port-au-Prince, June 25, 1991
Mr. Barber Conable
President of The World Bank
Washington, D.C.
et: Forestry and Environmental Protection Project
Dear Mr. President,
The priority which my Government places on the global increase of the
agricultural production implies a coherent policy towards the protection and
development of natural resources, and the management of the environment.
Within that policy the planned interventions have to build on agricultural
systems that are acceptable for the environment and viable for the people. In
this way the interventions would provide a valuable contribution towards the
improvement of the living conditions of the population without at the same
time causing a general decline of the quality of life in Haiti.
The analysis of the referenced project allows to confirm that the project
fits into this policy framework, and this is why my Government, through the
Ministry of Agriculture, Natural Resources and Rural Development (MARNDR),
places the highest priority on its implementation. In reality, both its
general and specific objectives reflect the Government's general strategy
towards the conservation of the natural resources and the protection of the
environment. That strategy aims to:
- Increase the protection and the value of the national forestry domain;
- Increase the availability of forest and agricultural products, and
thereby the income levels of the people in-olved in agroforestry;
- Ensure the preservation of rare and fragile ecosystems and of
endangered species; and
- Integrate the local communities in the programs to conserve and manage
natural resources, as well as to protect the environment.
My Government is committed to undertake all legal, institutional and
administrative measures needed to achieve the following specific objectives
within the time-frame indicated, and thanks to the efficient implementation of
the referenced projects
/ English Translation of the French original.



-227-
ANNX_ 10
Document 3
Page 2 of 2
- Increase tne forest cover of the country from 5% of its total area to
10% over a period of five years ;
- Manage and develop 15.000 ha of the national parks of Macaya and La
Visite within five years;
- Increase the national production of wood of all types by 50,000 mn/year
over five years, at a rate of 2 m'/ha;
- Ensure the preservation of habitats, species and genetic stock for
about thirty animal and plant species that are threatened by
extinction;
- Increase by 4% per year the agricultural production in the humid
mountains and the "buffer zones" around the national parks through
appropriate conservation practices; and
- Improve the legal and institutional framework to protect forests and
environment on a self-sustained basis.
In parallel, my Government is committed tot
* Carry out the classification of the forest resources and demarcation of
the national forest domain;
* Implement a system of forestry and environmental protection;
* Define a new legal framework through a review and update of the
existing legislation;
 Strengthen the administrative and institutional capacity through
appropriate measuresl and
* Mobilize external assistance for financing programs to protect forest
resources and the environment.
The finance requested from the International Development Association and
other similar institutions for the execution of the referenced project.
therefore, constitutes for my Government the basic initiative to implement its
policy for the protection of the natural resources and the environment.
Please accept, Mr. President, the assurance of my high consideration.
Signed: Rea6 Pr6val, Prime Miniater.
